TPz M.39/42

TPz M.40/44

Espingarda Mauser Modelo 1908
Mauser M08
The Mauser Model 1908 was an exceptionally long rifle based on the Mauser 1898 and chambered in 7x57mm Mauser. It was originally produced for Brazil by the German company DWM, but Brazil has subsequently begun to produce staggering quantities of the rifle domestically, and it became a staple in many state armies as time went it became almost synonym with the Interstate War in the cultural mindset of the nation. The Grão Pará state's army used the same gear as the other states that formed Brazil, and alongside the southern states it was the premier fighting force of the region.
Fusil Mauser Modelo 1935
Mauser M35
The Mauser Rifle Model 1935 is a unique Mauser Rifle which utilises a 7x57mm cartridge produced domestically by Brazil and abroad in close conjunction with the Mauser company, as part of the extensive collaboration between the Brazilian and German governments. Said collaboration started in the 1910s, as a clique officers then derided as 'the Young Turks' by the press began lobbying extensively for closer cooperation with the global hegemon. Everything from helmets to artillery pieces started to be fashioned after the German model, and as such a modernisation of the older Mausers became a priority. This firearm became almost synonymous with the Brazilian military during the Weltkrieg.
Submetralhadora Bergmann Modelo 1938
Bergmann M38
During the late years of the Pessoa presidency, Brazil purchased a relatively small number of Bergmann MP28 submachine guns, but they never saw much use outside of national guard regiments in Rio itself; The M38 is a simplified and easily manufactured derivative of the MP28 created in response to growing tensions in South America, and was intended to be the standard issue firearm for the entire Brazilian military and was produced in large quantities in foundries across the country and as such became almost synonymous with the Brazilian military during the Weltkrieg period.
Mosquetão Semi-Automático Rossi Modelo 1944
As the Second Weltkrieg raged in Europe, Brazilian leadership found that their weapons were simply outmatched as the arms race between the great powers, and rather than try to catch up, it simply started producing an outright copy of the newest German semi automatic rifle designs such as the G41. The first models were made in the Rossi foundry in Rio Grande do Sul, wherein the model takes its name from. While more advanced than other weapons in use by the Paulista army, its low reliability made it unpopular with soldiers, who often preferred older models or even captured gear.
Rossi M44
Metralhadora M.08/15 & Morteiro de 7,58-cm
The Weltkrieg saw the introduction of some of the deadliest weapons known to man, such as the fearsome M.G. Modell 08/15. In the close confines of trench warfare, the once archaic mortar was brought back to relevance. Many of these older piece of equipment were among the hardware directly sold or leased to Brazil over the course of the military mission from Germany in that nation.
Metralhadora M34 & Morteiro de 8cm
The Machine Gun Model 34 (MG34) was a recoil operated air-cooled machine gun. Chambered in the powerful 7.92x57-mm Mauser rifle cartridge, the machine gun's high rate of fire (900 RPM) and efficient mobility made it arguably the most advanced machine gun in the world at the time; and was eagerly shown to Brazil as a proof of the martial superiority of the Kaiserreich. The perhaps unexpected consequence of that was the fact that the design was copied with little regard for patents by the Rio government after Black Monday. The Mortar Model 34 is an infantry mortar that employs 8-cm shells. It has been noted for its high accuracy and rate of fire.
Metralhadora M39 & Morteiro 'Torneira' de 20cm
The Machine Gun Model 39 (M.G. 39) was an improvement over the M.G. 34. This belt fed machine gun features a high rate of fire and is suited for all purposes. The 20-cm leichter Mortar Model 40 is a spigot mortar designed for striking obstacles and strong points. Brazilian copies were noted for having inferior quality materials in their fabrication, presumably to lower costs.
Metralhadora Imbel M1 & Morteiro de 8cm
The first models of heavy machine guns produced by Brazil, for Brazil, the Imbel models were noted for very high rate of fire, but also relatively low reliability and durability due to several corners cut during production. When it was working properly, it was one of the most fearsome weapons in the country's arsenal, however.
Metralhadora Anti-Tanque Imbel .50
The first models of heavy machine guns produced by Brazil, for Brazil, the Imbel models were noted for very high rate of fire, but also relatively low reliability and durability due to several corners cut during production. When it was working properly, it was one of the most fearsome weapons in the country's arsenal, however. Heavier variants were assigned to Anti Tank duties in the lack of proper weapons for the task in the armed forces.
Lança Rojão M44
The Model 44 Rocket Launcher, like many Brazilian weapons of its time, is a rather uninspired copy of European equipment used in the Second Weltkrieg, later produced and used by Brazil itself. Based mostly on the German 'Panzerschreck', the weapon shares most specifications with it, including its 8,8-centimetre rockets and the massive smoke trail left by firing.
Obuseiro Rheinmetall de 10,5 cm
After a series of military missions from and to Germany, Brazil's government started to import foreign artillery from that nation. These were Rheinmetall models were relatively modern for their time, but the nation had a very limited capacity for mass production of these at home. These guns were a significant upgrade from previous models used by Germany especially when it comes to hitting moving targets.
Obuseiro Modelo 41 de 11,5cm
The Obuseiro Modelo 41 was little more than a copy of the specifications of the Modell 36 field howitzer from the German army, but with some tweaks to lower production costs, at the expense of some reliability. Even so, the gun was extensively used by artillery in Brazil across the Weltkrieg period, and was the first piece of artillery mass produced domestically itself rather than being mostly imported.
Obuseiro Modelo 43 de 17cm
Still following the trend of copying specifications from German pieces of equipment, the 1943 update of the M41 had a slight increase in firepower, and some refinements with the intent of making it more reliable. However, production was limited during wartime, and the upgraded field gun was a rare sight over the early 1940s. In later years, however, it and models with minimal variations were used by the Northern A until the 1960s.
Ob. Rm. de 10,5 cm
Ob. M41 de 11,5cm
Ob. M43 de 17cm
CAA M2 de 2cm
CAA M5 de 3,7cm
CAA M7 de 8,8cm
Canhão Antiaéreo M2 de 2cm
Anti Air weaponry was a defensive priority for Brazil, as the war between states had shown the vulnerabilities of the federal capital against aircraft despite extensive coastal and land based defences. Developed in conjunction and with support of Germany, the M1 and M2 were admittedly little more than copies of FlaK models from the early 1930s, but that was sufficient to deal with the relatively slow aircraft deployed in South America at the time.
Canhão Antiaéreo M5 de 3,7cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything from the main axle to the size of the cannon itself. The M5 is closely related to its German cousin, the 3,7cm Fla.K 37, but is made of lower quality materials to meet the production needs of Brazil.
Canhão Antiaéreo M7 de 8,8cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything, and the M7 is almost entirely a departure from the original design all but in role. The M7 was the first piece of anti aircraft equipment designed domestically, but even so it retained so many similarities to similar guns in use by Germany and its allies in the Weltkrieg many experts claim it wasn't a different gun at all, but rather a domestically produced variant. The cumbersome gun saw little use in Brazilian service before the war was over.
Canhão Antitanque 'Alemão' de 3,7 cm
Initially an outright import from Germany, the gun was straight up called 'Alemão' by the troops in the barracks due to its strength and obvious foreign origin, as its makers made sure to stamp imperial eagle effigies in seemingly every model. While similar models in Europe were designed to be drawn by motorised forces, Brazil used an outdated design more suited to the many horses it had available, and the lack of appropriate road infrastructure in an eventual frontline against Argentina. Said nation was the main motivator behind Brazil's move to start fabricating the piece domestically in small numbers after 1936, as the instability in that region, and having an unpredictable regime in possession of dozens of tanks, unnerved general staff in Rio.
Canhão Antitanque M2 de 7,5 cm
Developed entirely with German models as a basis, Anti Tank Artillery Model 2, or the AT2, was developed primarily against the types of heavier armour being used by European powers as the Second Weltkrieg raged. With Germany itself less than willing to cooperate with production this time, an illegally obtained piece was the basis for production domestically, which started much too late for widespread usage within the armed forces. The few models were usually distributed to divisions expected to face the burnt of enemy assaults.
Canhão Antitanque M6 de 12,8 cm
As the Weltkrieg came to a close, the armour in enemy Tanks became almost overbearing to infantry and older models of artillery alike, and Brazil's solution was yet again to mimic European designs in a cheap fashion. This domestic version of the Pa.K 43 has all of the problems of the original guns, but with lower reliability and was made with cheaper materials. Steel shortages were the main reason for the staggeringly low number of units produced during the war.
CAT Alemão de 3,7 cm
CAT de 7,5 cm
CAT M6 de 12,8 cm
Bateria Lança Foguetes de 32cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Following the specifications of the original models, rockets of 32cm were used in these weapons, and a lack of said rockets is exactly why they were seldom used.
Bateria Lança Foguetes de 21cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Changes to the size of the rockets required for usage made later models more common, but still far from standard issue and few pieces of the LF de 21cm were used in the field when compared to even the M43.
Bat. LF de 21cm
Bat. LF de 32cm
Class 238 Armstrong Whitworth 2-6-2
Class 1015 Alco-Schenectady 4-10-2

Espingarda 6,5 mm m/1904
Mauser-Vergueiro
A bolt action rifle with a long history of service, the 6,5 mm m/1904 is more commonly known by the moniker of 'Mauser-Vergueiro', as the main design of the gun is essentially that of the Mauser 98 (with a special bolt action mechanism heavily based on the Gewehr 1888) with the 6.5×58mm Vergueiro cartridge, one developed specifically for the Portuguese variant. It became the official weapon of the Portuguese Army in 1904 and continued in that capacity until the 1940s, being widely used both at home and overseas. While it was not used by Portuguese troops in the western front, as those were armed by Entente, chiefly British, supplies, it saw extensive use in Africa from Portugal, its allies in South Africa, and was also used by Germans who occasionally captured supplies in Portuguese East Africa.
Espingarda 7.92 mm m/1904/36
Mauser-Vergueiro M36
Fundamentally little more than an updated version of the Mauser-Vergueiro, it was introduced to Africa amid rising tensions between Portugal and Germany. A significant amount of these weapons later found themselves under use by the forces of the states in former Portuguese Africa.
Carabina C.A.P Modelo 01
C.A.P M40
A prototype carbine that saw little widespread use in Portugal's army as a whole was the country's first attempt at a standard issue, nationally produced design. As such, the nationalist Integralist junta supported the development of the C.A.P carbine intensely, but by the time it was ready for production, realities on the ground made it more worthwhile to switch to the FBP overall directly from the older Mauser-Vergueiro and variants already widely in use by the armed forces.
Pistola-Metralhadora FBP M/945
FBP M/945
The FBP submachine gun is named after the acronym for the Braço de Prata Factory, the manufacturer for the weapon, a foundry owned and controlled by the Portuguese government itself. While its development started far before its widespread use among foot troops in Portugal's army, the finished model entered production too late to replace the Mauser-Vergueiro in the war. This intent was made clear by the submachine gun having, rather unusually, bayonet sockets compatible with those of the Mauser models used by Portugal throughout the conflicts of the previous decades. The so called 'machine gun pistol' (The term in Portuguese for submachine guns in general) saw extensive use throughout many years, albeit the 1945 variant fell out of use in favour of versions with semi-automatic firing and imported models, as the scale of warfare decreased in later decades after the Second Weltkrieg. While few of the models made it overseas when compared to the old Mauser-Vergueiro, the government of Angola was capable of starting production domestically relatively swiftly after decolonisation.
Metralhadora Vickers & Morteiro Stokes
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the British Empire during the Weltkrieg. During its rearmament after the conflict, the restored Portuguese monarchy purchased and fabricated them with the intention of widespread use among infantry both at home and overseas.
Metralhadora Lewis Modelo DEMS & Morteiro SBML de 2 Polegadas
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis served the Entente since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. The MKIII variant had a pistol grip that allowed it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML, while small, is incredibly portable and had greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Metralhadora Vickers Modelo II & Morteiro ML de 3 Polegadas
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG featured a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It had been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar was a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it was reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Metralhadora Vickers-Vedras & Morteiro de 4.2 Polegadas
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar was a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.
Morteiro-Bombarda
Developed in Canada and sold in substantial amounts to Portugal, the mortar design was that of a 'spigot' mortar. Arguing that the design could serve as both an artillery piece and an anti-tank weapon, the design was eagerly adopted by several allies of the Entente, including Portugal. Many ended up stranded in its former overseas possessions.
LATI
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the LATI (A direct translation of its Canadian English name) was developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device was well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there were concerns about its intense recoil.

Mauser Modelo 1909
Mauser M-09
With its renowned Mauser 1891 design's shortcomings becoming increasingly glaring, Argentina placed an order for over 150,000 Mauser 1898 rifles in 7.65x53mm Mauser from German manufacturer DWM with later domestic production being equally as staggering.

Halcón M-43
Halcón M-43
A submachine gun of purely Argentine origin, the Halcon M-1943 was developed in 1941 by the Fabrica de Armas Halcon (Falcon Gun Factory) due to a need for submachine guns. Long employed by the police in Buenos Aires, the military has recently adopted the submachine gun. Military observers worldwide have compared the weapon to the American Thompson submachine gun, praising its reliability and rate of fire.

Hanyang Type 88
Hanyang Type 88
Originally a product of late Guangxu era 'Self Strengthening' efforts, the Type 88 was designed as a copy of the German Gewehr 88 with export modifications. While collectively inferior to newer rifles despite improvements, and often poorly maintained, the weapon is known to be rugged and reliable if unwieldy.\n\nThis variety is mainly produced at the Hanyang Arsenal, the model's place of design, though equipment shortages often see it used alongside non-standard weapons.
Hanyang Type 27
Hanyang Type 27
Officially called the 'Type 27', this rifle was put into service in 1935 thanks to German assistance after the Qing Restoration. The weapon is functionally a near copy of the German Mauser M1924, and a considerable improvement over the much older Type 88 in terms of weight and recoil.\n\nThis variety is mainly produced at the Hanyang Arsenal with German tools, though it may not fully replace its predecessors for some time.
Tsingtao MP18
Tsingtao MP18
Thanks to the flood of Weltkrieg surplus into the world market in the early 1920s, the MP18 saw action in China some time before a modified version began production in Tsingtao.\n\nWith changes to the magazine and stock, this particular product of Sino-German cooperation has been copied and sold throughout Northern and Southern China, though primarily to the Qing Government and warlords in coastal areas.
Hanyang Type 35
Hanyang Type 35
The Type 35 is a semi-automatic rifle based on the General Liu rifle from 1914. While appearing similar, the Type 35 is shorter, has the rear of the receiver covered, and the gas-trap system replaced by a simple gas port. The new rifle is also no longer able to switch to manual action and uses magazines from the Bohemian ZH-29 rifle and ZB-26 light machine gun.

Nanjing Type Zhongyuan
Zhongyuan
Following the Qing restoration, Benz AG and the Qing government established several automobile factories in China as joint ventures; the most famous being the Zhongyuan Automobile Plants located in Shanghai and Nanjing. Thousands of Type Zhongyuan trucks were produced in these two areas, which entered service in various armies loyal to the Zhili clique.\n\nNotably, the Zhongyuan is not named after the ancient Chinese place name Zhongyuan (the area surrounding the empire's core), but instead after the painted logo on the front of the truck's cab; a huge Chinese character 'Zhong' enclosed in a circle.

Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
LSAF SMLE Mk III
Lithgow Small Arms Factory in New South Wales opened in 1912, starting local production of the venerable Lee-Enfield rifle that would serve throughout the Great War. After the British Revolution, however, and the fall of large parts of British India, LSAF was the last remaining arsenal capable of producing Lee-Enfields to remain in Entente hands, making it a very important strategic asset.
Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk VI
LSAF SMLE Mk VI
In cooperation with British-Canadian engineers and the Lithgow factory, the Lee-Enfield Mk III was modernised with new and improved aperture sights on the receiver instead of at the mid-point of the rifle, becoming the new standard for the Dominions. However, experience in jungle warfare brought to attention new needs for the service rifle, specifically in weight, length, and handling. Lithgow thus developed the \
Carbine, Machine, Owen Mk I
Owen Gun
With the looming war and experience during the war in the US, the Entente armies grew increasingly interested in a mass-producible submachine-gun. Evelyn Owen, a private in the Australasian Army and hobbyist gunsmith, caught the attention of both Canadian and Australasian armies with a design of his own. The Owen gun was a very simple, but unorthodox design with its top-loaded magazine and two vertical grips. Not only is it relatively inexpensive, but it proved to be extremely rugged, surviving in even the worst of conditions, earning it the nickname \
Rifle, Self-Loading, Long Branch Mk I
SLR Mk I
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-tank No. 68
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector Infantry Tank Attack Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector Infantry Tank Attack (PITA, known as Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank/PIAT in other Dominions) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.

Ruskin Rhino
Rhino
With the success of the Rover under its belt, the Ruskin Motor Bodies company designed the Rhino in response to an Australasian government request for a substantially heavier armoured car built upon the CMP chassis. Though substantial changes were made to that CMP chassis, after much work the Rhino was born, and with substantial interchangeability of parts with other CMP-based designs to boot.
Ruskin Hippo
Hippo
To accommodate a substantially larger gun, the Rhino's coaxial machine had to be removed and its turret slightly enlarged. The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

Maschinenpistole Modell 1934
Steyr-Solothurn M.34
While a German design by Louis Stange, the  MP 34 was mainly produced by Steyr and was adopted by the Austro-Hungarian Army. As common with early submachine guns, this design was heavy, expensive, but reliable, with a horizontal magazine using the potent 9x25mm Mauser cartridge.
Maschinenpistole Modell 38
Zbrojovka Brno M.38
Seeing the need for a submachine-gun using the standard pistol cartridge of the Army, the Monarchy would adopt the ZK 383, designed by brothers Josef and František Koucký at Zbrojovka Brno, as the MP 38. Using a horizontal magazine like the MP 34, the ZK 383 is known for having a light bipod, allowing it to be used as a substitute squad automatic weapon. Later models were heavily simplified for cheaper and faster production, along with a vertical foldable magazine like the Danuvia SMGs.

These made them one of the first modern armoured personnel carriers and significantly lengthening the service life of its parent design.

k.k. Staatsbahnen 581
kkStB 581
Built shortly after the Great War, the kkStB 581 was a class of 2-10-0 locomotives designed to pull heavy coal trains from Moravia and Silesia. They were equipped with two cylinders and a superheater to produce the large amount of power needed. Later the true power of Rihorsk's design would be realised with a four cylinder one-off.
k.k. Staatsbahnen 552
kkStB 552
A near copy of the Prussian state railways G 13 locomotive, these locomotives have a lighter axle load, higher top speed and were cheaper to produce compared to the kkStB 552, but could pull the same amount of freight. They would quickly replace most freight locomotives on the Austro-Hungarian railway network.

Espingarda Mauser Modelo 1908
Mauser M08
The Mauser Model 1908 was an exceptionally long rifle based on the Mauser 1898 and chambered in 7x57mm Mauser. It was originally produced for Brazil by the German company DWM, but Brazil has subsequently begun to produce staggering quantities of the rifle domestically, and it became a staple in many state armies as time went it became almost synonym with the Interstate War in the cultural mindset of the nation. The Bahia state's army used the same gear as the other states that formed Brazil, and alongside the southern states it was the premier fighting force of the region.
Fusil Mauser Modelo 1935
Mauser M35
The Mauser Rifle Model 1935 is a unique Mauser Rifle which utilises a 7x57mm cartridge produced domestically by Brazil and abroad in close conjunction with the Mauser company, as part of the extensive collaboration between the Brazilian and German governments. Said collaboration started in the 1910s, as a clique officers then derided as 'the Young Turks' by the press began lobbying extensively for closer cooperation with the global hegemon. Everything from helmets to artillery pieces started to be fashioned after the German model, and as such a modernisation of the older Mausers became a priority. This firearm became almost synonymous with the Brazilian military during the Weltkrieg.
Submetralhadora Bergmann Modelo 1938
Bergmann M38
During the late years of the Pessoa presidency, Brazil purchased a relatively small number of Bergmann MP28 submachine guns, but they never saw much use outside of national guard regiments in Rio itself; The M38 is a simplified and easily manufactured derivative of the MP28 created in response to growing tensions in South America, and was intended to be the standard issue firearm for the entire Brazilian military and was produced in large quantities in foundries across the country and as such became almost synonymous with the Brazilian military during the Weltkrieg period.
Mosquetão Semi-Automático Rossi Modelo 1944
As the Second Weltkrieg raged in Europe, Brazilian leadership found that their weapons were simply outmatched as the arms race between the great powers, and rather than try to catch up, it simply started producing an outright copy of the newest German semi automatic rifle designs such as the G41. The first models were made in the Rossi foundry in Rio Grande do Sul, wherein the model takes its name from. While more advanced than other weapons in use by the Paulista army, its low reliability made it unpopular with soldiers, who often preferred older models or even captured gear.
Rossi M44
Metralhadora M.08/15 & Morteiro de 7,58-cm
The Weltkrieg saw the introduction of some of the deadliest weapons known to man, such as the fearsome M.G. Modell 08/15. In the close confines of trench warfare, the once archaic mortar was brought back to relevance. Many of these older piece of equipment were among the hardware directly sold or leased to Brazil over the course of the military mission from Germany in that nation.
Metralhadora M34 & Morteiro de 8cm
The Machine Gun Model 34 (MG34) was a recoil operated air-cooled machine gun. Chambered in the powerful 7.92x57-mm Mauser rifle cartridge, the machine gun's high rate of fire (900 RPM) and efficient mobility made it arguably the most advanced machine gun in the world at the time; and was eagerly shown to Brazil as a proof of the martial superiority of the Kaiserreich. The perhaps unexpected consequence of that was the fact that the design was copied with little regard for patents by the Rio government after Black Monday. The Mortar Model 34 is an infantry mortar that employs 8-cm shells. It has been noted for its high accuracy and rate of fire.
Metralhadora M39 & Morteiro 'Torneira' de 20cm
The Machine Gun Model 39 (M.G. 39) was an improvement over the M.G. 34. This belt fed machine gun features a high rate of fire and is suited for all purposes. The 20-cm leichter Mortar Model 40 is a spigot mortar designed for striking obstacles and strong points. Brazilian copies were noted for having inferior quality materials in their fabrication, presumably to lower costs.
Metralhadora Imbel M1 & Morteiro de 8cm
The first models of heavy machine guns produced by Brazil, for Brazil, the Imbel models were noted for very high rate of fire, but also relatively low reliability and durability due to several corners cut during production. When it was working properly, it was one of the most fearsome weapons in the country's arsenal, however.
Metralhadora Anti-Tanque Imbel .50
The first models of heavy machine guns produced by Brazil, for Brazil, the Imbel models were noted for very high rate of fire, but also relatively low reliability and durability due to several corners cut during production. When it was working properly, it was one of the most fearsome weapons in the country's arsenal, however. Heavier variants were assigned to Anti Tank duties in the lack of proper weapons for the task in the armed forces.
Lança Rojão M44
The Model 44 Rocket Launcher, like many Brazilian weapons of its time, is a rather uninspired copy of European equipment used in the Second Weltkrieg, later produced and used by Brazil itself. Based mostly on the German 'Panzerschreck', the weapon shares most specifications with it, including its 8,8-centimetre rockets and the massive smoke trail left by firing.
Obuseiro Rheinmetall de 10,5 cm
After a series of military missions from and to Germany, Brazil's government started to import foreign artillery from that nation. These were Rheinmetall models were relatively modern for their time, but the nation had a very limited capacity for mass production of these at home. These guns were a significant upgrade from previous models used by Germany especially when it comes to hitting moving targets.
Obuseiro Modelo 41 de 11,5cm
The Obuseiro Modelo 41 was little more than a copy of the specifications of the Modell 36 field howitzer from the German army, but with some tweaks to lower production costs, at the expense of some reliability. Even so, the gun was extensively used by artillery in Brazil across the Weltkrieg period, and was the first piece of artillery mass produced domestically itself rather than being mostly imported.
Obuseiro Modelo 43 de 17cm
Still following the trend of copying specifications from German pieces of equipment, the 1943 update of the M41 had a slight increase in firepower, and some refinements with the intent of making it more reliable. However, production was limited during wartime, and the upgraded field gun was a rare sight over the early 1940s. In later years, however, it and models with minimal variations were used by the Brazilian army until the 1960s.

Canhão Antiaéreo M2 de 2cm
Anti Air weaponry was a defensive priority for Brazil, as the war between states had shown the vulnerabilities of the federal capital against aircraft despite extensive coastal and land based defences. Developed in conjunction and with support of Germany, the M1 and M2 were admittedly little more than copies of FlaK models from the early 1930s, but that was sufficient to deal with the relatively slow aircraft deployed in South America at the time.
Canhão Antiaéreo M5 de 3,7cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything from the main axle to the size of the cannon itself. The M5 is closely related to its German cousin, the 3,7cm Fla.K 37, but is made of lower quality materials to meet the production needs of Brazil.
Canhão Antiaéreo M7 de 8,8cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything, and the M7 is almost entirely a departure from the original design all but in role. The M7 was the first piece of anti aircraft equipment designed domestically, but even so it retained so many similarities to similar guns in use by Germany and its allies in the Weltkrieg many experts claim it wasn't a different gun at all, but rather a domestically produced variant. The cumbersome gun saw little use in Brazilian service before the war was over.
Canhão Antitanque 'Alemão' de 3,7 cm
Initially an outright import from Germany, the gun was straight up called 'Alemão' by the troops in the barracks due to its strength and obvious foreign origin, as its makers made sure to stamp imperial eagle effigies in seemingly every model. While similar models in Europe were designed to be drawn by motorised forces, Brazil used an outdated design more suited to the many horses it had available, and the lack of appropriate road infrastructure in an eventual frontline against Argentina. Said nation was the main motivator behind Brazil's move to start fabricating the piece domestically in small numbers after 1936, as the instability in that region, and having an unpredictable regime in possession of dozens of tanks, unnerved general staff in Rio.
Canhão Antitanque M2 de 7,5 cm
Developed entirely with German models as a basis, Anti Tank Artillery Model 2, or the AT2, was developed primarily against the types of heavier armour being used by European powers as the Second Weltkrieg raged. With Germany itself less than willing to cooperate with production this time, an illegally obtained piece was the basis for production domestically, which started much too late for widespread usage within the armed forces. The few models were usually distributed to divisions expected to face the burnt of enemy assaults.
Canhão Antitanque M6 de 12,8 cm
As the Weltkrieg came to a close, the armour in enemy Tanks became almost overbearing to infantry and older models of artillery alike, and Brazil's solution was yet again to mimic European designs in a cheap fashion. This domestic version of the Pa.K 43 has all of the problems of the original guns, but with lower reliability and was made with cheaper materials. Steel shortages were the main reason for the staggeringly low number of units produced during the war.
CAT Alemão de 3,7 cm

Bateria Lança Foguetes de 32cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Following the specifications of the original models, rockets of 32cm were used in these weapons, and a lack of said rockets is exactly why they were seldom used.
Bateria Lança Foguetes de 21cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Changes to the size of the rockets required for usage made later models more common, but still far from standard issue and few pieces of the LF de 21cm were used in the field when compared to even the M43.

Maschinenkarabiner 42
MKb 42
The MKb 42 is a revolutionary design chambered for a new intermediate cartridge, developed by famous German arms designer Hugo Schmeisser in conjunction with the Haenel company. While the first prototype of the rifle was prone to jamming, both the military and even the Kaiser himself were extremely enthusiastic about the project and immense resources were concentrated on improving the design, with the rifle deemed ready for adoption in 1942.

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Sd.Kfz. 11
The Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11 was developed from 1934 to 1938 and involved Borgward AG and, from 1937 onwards, the design became associated with Hannoversche Maschinenbau AG. Initially intended to be a prime mover, the Sd.Kfz. 11 soon was transferred to the role of a transporter, when the need for an all-terrain vehicle became apparent. Capable of carrying eight soldiers in addition to towing a gun or a trailer, the truck became the main vehicle of panzergrenadiers.

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 256
Sd.Kfz. 256
In late 1943, the German Army asked Auto-Union to develop a light, fully tracked reconnaissance vehicle, capable of carrying 6-8 men in an open-top superstructure. Introduced in 1944 the Schwerer Schützenpanzerwagen 'Kätzchen' (Heavy Armoured Personnel Carrier 'Kitten') had the hull's shape similar to the hull of the Stu.Pz.Kpfw. VII, but much smaller. The vehicle has front wheel drive with five or six overlapping steel road wheels and was designed to attain speeds in excess of 50 km/hr and has light armour. Warmly met by the German Military despite not very high speed, the vehicle was adopted in service under the name Sd.Kfz. 256.

10,5-cm leichte Feldhaubitze Modell 33
10,5-cm le.F.H. 33
The 10,5-cm le.F.H. 33 is the standard artillery piece of the Deutsches Heer, adopted for service in 1933 and used by all divisions and artillery battalions. This gun was developed in 1928-29 by Rheinmetall, and was a major advance on its predecessor the 10,5-cm le.F.H. 16 - a completely new three-point split trail gun carriage provides more stability and increases the traverse to 56 degrees alongside with the sighting mechanism to make it easier to fire at moving targets.
15-cm schwere Feldhaubitze Modell 36
15-cm s.F.H. 36
The 15-cm schwere Feldhaubitze 36, nicknamed Immergrün ('Evergreen'), is the basic division-level heavy howitzer, serving alongside the smaller but more numerous 10,5-cm le.F.H. 33. The gun originated with a contest between Rheinmetall and Krupp, both of whom entered several designs that were all considered unsatisfactory for one reason or another. In the end the army decided the solution was to combine the best features of both designs, using the Rheinmetall gun on a Krupp carriage. In 1935 the Army drafted a requirement for a lightened version of the gun which could be towed by a single horse team in one piece, for which a new proposed design from Rheinmetall was selected.
17-cm Kanone Modell 40 in Mörserlafette
17-cm K. 40 Mrs.Laf.
The 17-cm Kanone 40, built by Krupp and Hannoversche Maschinenbau, is heavy corps-level gun. In the field, the gun delivers great damage, but also has a great disadvantage of requiring a long period of time to tear down for transport. A notable innovation on the 17-cm K. 40 is the \
3,7-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 36
3,7-cm Pa.K. 36
Design of a horse-drawn, 3,7-cm anti-tank gun (designated 3,7-cm Pa.K. 28) by Rheinmetall commenced in 1924. By the early 1930s, it became apparent that horse-drawn artillery is obsolete, and the gun was modified for motorised transport, allowing it to be towed at highway speeds. Nicknamed by soldiers 'Heeresanklopfgerät' ('army door-knocking device'), the re-designated 3,7-cm Pa.K. 36 is the main anti-tank weapon of Deutsches Heer and easily can manage with enemy light tanks.
7,5-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 40
7,5-cm Pa.K. 40
The 7,5-cm Pa.K. 40 was developed after reports of new enemy tank designs began to reach Berlin in 1939. The project was initially given low priority, but following the growing tensions with France and the appearance of heavily armoured tanks, it was given an increased priority. In terms of armour-piercing action, the Pa.K. 40 is superior to the similar guns, but have less effective rollback damping systems, as a result of which the gun is behind analogues to quickly change position.
12,8-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 43
12,8-cm Pa.K. 43
The 12,8-cm Pa.K. 43 is the new anti-tank gun developed initially as a field gun known as the Kanone 43. However, once heavier tanks started to appear the design requirements were altered to include an anti-armour role. The 12,8-cm Pa.K. 44 has a good anti-tank performance over long to extreme-long ranges - 1,800-2,700 metres and beyond - while also doubling as an effective field gun when firing high-explosive shells. But since it was not possible to install a 12,8-cm barrel on the carriage of a conventional anti-tank gun, a special carriage for the gun was designed. The high profile of the gun had to be maintained, which makes the gun extremely visible.
2-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 36
2-cm Fla.K. 36
The 2-cm Fla.K. 30 automatic anti-aircraft cannon was developed by Rheinmetall in 1930, and the Deutsches Heer began to receive guns in 1933. In 1936, due to the growth of tensions, Mauser modernised the gun - the upgraded model received the designation 2-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone 36 and was adopted by the German army. The principle of operation of the Fla.K. 36 mechanisms remains the same - the use of recoil force with a short barrel stroke, but otherwise is similar with only increased the rate of fire to 220 rounds per minute and slightly lowered overall weight to 420 kg.
3,7-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 37
3,7-cm Fla.K. 37
The original 3,7-cm gun was developed by Rheinmetall in 1935. The cannon had an overall length of 89 calibres, which allowed a 4,800 metres maximum ceiling. The Fla.K. 35 was only produced in small numbers, and production had already ended in 1936. Development continued, focusing on replacement of the existing cumbersome dual-axle mount with a lighter single-axle one, resulting in a 3,7-cm Fla.K. 36 with the practical rate of fire raised to 120 rounds per minute. A new, simplified sighting system introduced the next year led to the otherwise-identical 3,7-cm Fla.K. 37 which quickly became the main variant of the gun for the German Army.
8,8-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 41
8,8-cm Fla.K. 41
Initially developed in 1930 by Krupp the 8,8-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone 30, commonly known as 'acht-acht' ('eight-eight'), was later modified by Rheinmetall. The versatile carriage allows the 8,8-cm Fla.K. to fire in a limited anti-tank mode when still on its wheels, and many consider the 8,8-cm Fla.K. line to be one of the best of its time. The Rheinmetall Fla.K. 41 is a second generation 8,8-cm gun, which some generals say is 'almost equal to the 12,8-cm cannon'. While having good characteristics, the Fla.K. 41 has problems in service, and is used mainly where it can be properly maintained.
28/32-cm Schweres Wurfgerät Modell 40
s.W.G. 40
Initially designed for Nebeltruppen (Smoke Troops) for smokescreen setting, the schwere Wurfgerät 40 exists in two variants - with wooden and steel frames. The open frames of the most common steel version have short hinged legs to adjust elevation and are sized to fit the 32-centimetre rocket, but adapter rails are provided to allow the 28-centimetre rockets to fit. The s.W.G. 40 also can be mounted on schwere Wurfrahmen 40 frames attached to the sides of different vehicles.
21-cm Raketenwerfer Modell 42
R.W. 42
The 21-cm Raketenwerfer 42 is a five-barreled multiple rocket launcher mounted on the towed carriage derived from that of the 3,7 cm Pa.K. 36 anti-tank gun. The launchers are organised into batteries of six with three batteries per battalion. Intended to provide the fire support and settle smokescreens, the R.W. 42 are mainly used as rocket artillery and almost don't participate in chemical warfare.

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Sd.Kfz. 11
The Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11 was developed from 1934 to 1938 and involved Borgward AG and, from 1937 onwards, the design became associated with Hannoversche Maschinenbau AG. Initially intended to be a prime mover, the Sd.Kfz. 11 soon was transferred to the role of a transporter, when the need for an all-terrain vehicle became apparent. Capable of carrying eight soldiers in addition to towing a gun or a trailer, the truck became the main vehicle of panzergrenadiers.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251
Sd.Kfz. 251
Developed on the chassis of Sd.Kfz. 11, the Schützenpanzerwagen Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251 half-tracks (commonly referred to as \
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 256
Sd.Kfz. 256
In late 1943, the German Army asked Auto-Union to develop a light, fully tracked reconnaissance vehicle, capable of carrying 6-8 men in an open-top superstructure. Introduced in 1944 the Schwerer Schützenpanzerwagen 'Kätzchen' (Heavy Armoured Personnel Carrier 'Kitten') had the hull's shape similar to the hull of the Stu.Pz.Kpfw. VII, but much smaller. The vehicle has front wheel drive with five or six overlapping steel road wheels and was designed to attain speeds in excess of 50 km/hr and has light armour. Warmly met by the German Military despite not very high speed, the vehicle was adopted in service under the name Sd.Kfz. 256.

SNCB Type 64
SNCB Type 64
Originally built as the Prussian P 8 for the Prussian state railways, the Type 64 were purchased from Germany after the Great War as the newly reorganised Flanders-Wallonia found themselves lacking suitable rolling stock for mixed traffic service. Later, a licence to produce them domestically was acquired, allowing the production of the Type 64 locally.
SNCB Type 1
SNCB Type 1
Built for heavy express passenger services, the Type 1 locomotives were not prepared for war time freight service. However, the lack of an alternative meant that they were pushed into freight service regardless. The locomotive would see heavy usage around the Belgian network, supplementing the other German locomotives acquired from Germany.

Ishapore SMLE Mk III
Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
The Ishapore Rifle Factory was founded in 1904 and began producing the venerable Lee-Enfield (SMLE) rifle that would serve throughout the Great War. However, the loss of much of British India after the British Revolution also meant the loss of the Ishapore factory, one of the only two factories producing the SMLE outside of Britain. Many guns produced prior to the revolution still however remain in service, and more still are produced in and surrounding the former lands of the Raj.

Mauser Modelo 1908
Modelo 1908
As tensions mounted in the Gran Chaco in the beginning of the 20th century, Bolivia and Paraguay each commenced audacious rearmament campaigns to counter the other. During this period, Bolivia adopted a derivative of the Mauser 1898 designed for interchangeability with older Bolivian Mauser 1891 rifles. This rifle, chambered in 7.65×53mm Mauser, became the backbone of the Bolivian military and demand for domestically produced rifles sparked rapid growth in the Bolivian arms industry.
Zbrojovka Brno Mauser Modelo 1928
Modelo 1928
With unrest increasingly threatening the stability of its neighbours, in the late 1920s, Bolivia saw the need to again update its infantry armaments. Ultimately, Bolivia selected a derivative of the Dual Monarchy's Brno version 1924 carbine chambered in 7.65×53mm Mauser.

Karabin Model 1895/30
Mannlicher M.95/30
The Mannlicher Karabin Model 1895/1930 (German: Infanterie Repetier-Gewehr M.95) was the service rifle of the Austro-Hungarian armed forces during the Weltkrieg. Designed by Ferdinand von Mannlicher, it used a straight-pull bolt-action for increased rate-of-fire compared to its contemporaries, giving it the nickname Ruck-Zuck (German slang for \
Karabin Model 1935
FÉG M.35
The Weltkrieg made the M.95's deficiencies all-too obvious, especially its relatively underpowered round-nose cartridge and the required maintenance for the straight-pull action. Steyr developed the 8x56mm \

Strojokarabin Model 1942

As it became clear that engagement distances had significantly shortened since the Weltkrieg, the Monarchy became interested in developing an intermediate cartridge and the \
Kratka strojnica Model 1934
Steyr-Solothurn M.34
While a German design by Louis Stange, the  MP 34 was mainly produced by Steyr and was adopted by the Austro-Hungarian Army. As common with early submachine guns, this design was heavy, expensive, but reliable, with a horizontal magazine using the potent 9x25mm Mauser cartridge.
Kratka strojnica Model 38
Zbrojovka Brno M.38
Seeing the need for a submachine-gun using the standard pistol cartridge of the Army, the Monarchy would adopt the ZK 383, designed by brothers Josef and František Koucký at Zbrojovka Brno, as the MP 38. Using a horizontal magazine like the MP 34, the ZK 383 is known for having a light bipod, allowing it to be used as a substitute squad automatic weapon. Later models were heavily simplified for cheaper and faster production, along with a vertical foldable magazine like the Danuvia SMGs.
Kratka strojnica Model 1943
Danuvia M.43
While the Danuvia 39M proved successful, its folding magazine was not enough to compensate for its bulky design for vehicle crewmen. The 43M redesign with a folding metal stock and a new, more reliable foldable magazine design allowed it to be much more compact during transport or storage, without sacrificing firepower.

Espingarda Mauser Modelo 1908
Espingarda Mauser M08
The Mauser Model 1908 is an exceptionally long rifle based on the Mauser 1898 and chambered in 7x57mm Mauser. It was originally produced for Brazil by the German company DWM, but Brazil has subsequently begun to produce staggering quantities of the rifle domestically.
Fusil Mauser modelo 1935
Fusil m35
The Mauser Rifle Model 1935 is a unique Mauser Rifle which utilises a 7x57mm cartridge produced domestically by Brazil and abroad in close conjunction with the Mauser company, as part of the extensive collaboration between the Brazilian and German governments. Said collaboration started in the 1910s, as a clique officers then derided as 'the Young Turks' by the press began lobbying extensively for closer cooperation with the global hegemon. Everything from helmets to artillery pieces started to be fashioned after the German model, and as such a modernisation of the older Mausers quickly became a priority for the government.
Bergmann Submetralhadora Modelo 1938
Bergmann M38
During the late years of the Pessoa presidency, Brazil purchased a relatively small number of Bergmann MP28 submachine guns, but they never saw much use outside of national guard regiments in Rio itself; The M38 is a simplified and easily manufactured derivative of the MP28 created in response to growing tensions in South America, and was intended to be the standard issue firearm for the entire Brazilian military and was produced in large quantities in foundries across the country and as such became almost synonymous with the Brazilian military during the Weltkrieg period.
Mosquetão Semi-Automático Rossi Modelo 1944
Rossi M44
As the Second Weltkrieg raged in Europe, Brazilian leadership found that their weapons were simply outmatched as the arms race between the great powers, and rather than try to catch up, it simply started producing an outright copy of the newest German semi automatic rifle designs such as the G41. The first models were made in the Rossi foundry in Rio Grande do Sul, wherein the model takes its name from. While more advanced than other weapons in use by the Brazilian army, its low reliability made it unpopular with soldiers, who often preferred older models or even captured gear..
Metralhadora M.08/15 & Morteiro de 7,58-cm
The Weltkrieg saw the introduction of some of the deadliest weapons known to man, such as the fearsome MG. Model 08/15. In the close confines of trench warfare, the once archaic mortar was brought back to relevance and found newfound efficiency. Many such older pieces of equipment were among the hardware directly sold or leased to Brazil over the course of the military mission from Germany in that nation.
Metralhadora M34 & Morteiro de 8cm
The Machine Gun Model 34 (MG34) was a recoil operated air-cooled machine gun. Chambered in the powerful 7.92x57-mm Mauser rifle cartridge, the machine gun's high rate of fire (900 RPM) and efficient mobility made it arguably the most advanced machine gun in the world at the time; and was eagerly shown to Brazil as a proof of the martial superiority of the Kaiserreich. The perhaps unexpected consequence of that was the fact that the design was copied with little regard for patents by the Rio government after Black Monday. The Mortar Model 34 is an infantry mortar that employs 8-cm shells. It has been noted for its high accuracy and rate of fire.
Metralhadora M39 & Morteiro 'Torneira' de 20cm
The Machine Gun Model 39 (MG39) was an improvement over the MG34. This belt fed machine gun features a high rate of fire and is suited for all purposes. The 20-cm leichter Mortar Model 40 is a spigot mortar designed for striking obstacles and strong points. Brazilian copies were noted for having inferior quality materials in their fabrication, presumably to lower costs.
Metralhadora Imbel M1 & Morteiro de 8cm
The first models of heavy machine guns produced by Brazil, for Brazil, the Imbel models were noted for very high rate of fire, but also relatively low reliability and durability due to several corners cut during production. When it was working properly, it was one of the most fearsome weapons in the country's arsenal, however.
Metralhadora Anti-Tanque Imbel .50
The Model 44 Rocket Launcher, like many Brazilian weapons of its time, is a rather uninspired copy of European equipment used in the Second Weltkrieg, later produced and used by Brazil itself. Based mostly on the German 'Panzerschreck', the weapon shares most specifications with it, including its 8,8-centimetre rockets and the massive smoke trail left by firing.
Lança Rojão M44
The Model 44 Rocket Launcher, like many Brazilian weapons of its time, is a rather uninspired copy of European equipment used in the Second Weltkrieg, later produced and used by Brazil itself. Based mostly on the German 'Panzerschreck', the weapon shares most specifications with it, including its 8,8-centimetre rockets and the massive smoke trail left by firing.
Obuseiro Rheinmetall de 10,5 cm
After a series of military missions from and to Germany, Brazil's government started to import foreign artillery from that nation. These were Rheinmetall models were relatively modern for their time, but the nation had a very limited capacity for mass production of these at home. These guns were a significant upgrade from previous models used by Germany especially when it comes to hitting moving targets.
Obuseiro Modelo 41 de 11,5cm
The Obuseiro Modelo 41 was little more than a copy of the specifications of the Modell 36 field howitzer from the German army, but with some tweaks to lower production costs, at the expense of some reliability. Even so, the gun was extensively used by artillery in Brazil across the Weltkrieg period, and was the first piece of artillery mass produced domestically itself rather than being mostly imported.
Obuseiro Modelo 43 de 17cm
Still following the trend of copying specifications from German pieces of equipment, the 1943 update of the M41 had a slight increase in firepower, and some refinements with the intent of making it more reliable. However, production was limited during wartime, and the upgraded field gun was a rare sight over the early 1940s. In later years, however, it and models with minimal variations were used by the Brazilian army until the 1960s.
Ob. Rm. de 10,5 cm
Ob. M41 de 11,5cm
Ob. M43 de 17cm
CAA M2 de 2cm
CAA M5 de 3,7cm
CAA M7 de 8,8cm
Canhão Antiaéreo M2 de 2cm
Anti Air weaponry was a defensive priority for Brazil, as the war between states had shown the vulnerabilities of the federal capital against aircraft despite extensive coastal and land based defences. Developed in conjunction and with support of Germany, the M1 and M2 were admittedly little more than copies of FlaK models from the early 1930s, but that was sufficient to deal with the relatively slow aircraft deployed in South America at the time.
Canhão Antiaéreo M5 de 3,7cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything from the main axle to the size of the cannon itself. The M5 is closely related to its German cousin, the 3,7cm Fla.K 37, but is made of lower quality materials to meet the production needs of Brazil.
Canhão Antiaéreo M7 de 8,8cm
As aircraft rapidly developed, several updates and changes to the standard issue M2 were made over the years, but later models of anti aircraft weaponry entirely different specifications regarding almost everything, and the M7 is almost entirely a departure from the original design all but in role. The M7 was the first piece of anti aircraft equipment designed domestically, but even so it retained so many similarities to similar guns in use by Germany and its allies in the Weltkrieg many experts claim it wasn't a different gun at all, but rather a domestically produced variant. The cumbersome gun saw little use in Brazilian service before the war was over.
Canhão Antitanque 'Alemão' de 3,7 cm
Initially an outright import from Germany, the gun was straight up called 'Alemão' by the troops in the barracks due to its strength and obvious foreign origin, as its makers made sure to stamp imperial eagle effigies in seemingly every model. While similar models in Europe were designed to be drawn by motorised forces, Brazil used an outdated design more suited to the many horses it had available, and the lack of appropriate road infrastructure in an eventual frontline against Argentina. Said nation was the main motivator behind Brazil's move to start fabricating the piece domestically in small numbers after 1936, as the instability in that region, and having an unpredictable regime in possession of dozens of tanks, unnerved general staff in Rio.
Canhão Antitanque M2 de 7,5 cm
Developed entirely with German models as a basis, Anti Tank Artillery Model 2, or the AT2, was developed primarily against the types of heavier armour being used by European powers as the Second Weltkrieg raged. With Germany itself less than willing to cooperate with production this time, an illegally obtained piece was the basis for production domestically, which started much too late for widespread usage within the armed forces. The few models were usually distributed to divisions expected to face the burnt of enemy assaults.
Canhão Antitanque M6 de 12,8 cm
As the Weltkrieg came to a close, the armour in enemy Tanks became almost overbearing to infantry and older models of artillery alike, and Brazil's solution was yet again to mimic European designs in a cheap fashion. This domestic version of the Pa.K 43 has all of the problems of the original guns, but with lower reliability and was made with cheaper materials. Steel shortages were the main reason for the staggeringly low number of units produced during the war.
CAT Alemão de 3,7 cm
CAT de 7,5 cm
CAT M6 de 12,8 cm
Bateria Lança Foguetes de 32cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Following the specifications of the original models, rockets of 32cm were used in these weapons, and a lack of said rockets is exactly why they were seldom used.
Bateria Lança Foguetes de 21cm
Rocket artillery was rarely utilised by the Brazilian military during the war period, and the few pieces manufactured were direct copies of what little German equipment of the type Brazilian officers got their hands on. Changes to the size of the rockets required for usage made later models more common, but still far from standard issue and few pieces of the LF de 21cm were used in the field when compared to even the M43.
Bat. LF de 21cm
Bat. LF de 32cm

Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
SMLE Mk III
The venerable Lee-Enfield (SMLE) rifle that served the Empire throughout the Great War continues to be the standard arm of the Dominions following the Empire's collapse. Quantities of the SMLE rifle can be found in arsenal throughout the many Dominions. With rifle production seriously disrupted with the loss of the arms factories of England, the Dominions have turned to the old reliable three-oh-three as their standard weapon of choice until suitable replacements are created or purchased.
SMLE Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the need to rearm what remained of the Empire became paramount. British engineers from the RSAF (Royal Small Arms Factory) began taking refuge in Ontario to work on an existing Lee-Enfield. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the side instead of the middle, which features improved sighting radius, sighting accuracy, and sighting speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.
Owen Mk 1
Owen Machine Carbine
With the prospect of war coming, the Army has requested a submachine gun to be developed for close quarters and urban warfare, especially with the destructiveness that characterises the civil war in America. The Canadian Army officially adopted a weapon designed by young Australian private Evelyn Owen. The 
Long Branch Mk 1
SLR Mk 1
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat. Many copies were sent to arm the Dominions of the Empire in preparation for the eventual homecoming.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-tank No. 68
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I (PIAT) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the Canadian Army during the Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the Canadian Army has recommissioned them back into service.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Vickers M Mk II & ML 3 inch mortar
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG features a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It has been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar is a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it is reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Vickers M Mk III & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar is a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.
Tabby Scope
The Tabby Scope is one of the Canadian government's most closesly guarded secrets; the process of development is even hidden from the fellow members of the Entente. A chance discovery by scientists in the ISAC, the scope is shaped in the form of a canteen but contains a monocular infrared scope along with a battery. Assigned to only the most elite of the elite, the scope allows the unprecedented advantage of having vision in the dark.
BSA Thompson M1926
The Canadian Army has formally adopted the iconic American submachine gun associated with crime and vice. The M1928A1 is the service variant of the classic M1921 Thompson Machine gun, and although production is expensive, the gun is well-liked among the troops in the field who praise its efficiency and reliability. While most variants rely on smaller 'box' magazines, some other variants feature large and bulky fifty-round drum magazines.
Lanchester Submachine Gun
With the outbreak of all out war in the United States and the looming clouds of war in Europe, the Canadian Army desperately needs arms for the homecoming. With little time to prepare and research, the Canadian Army has commissioned the 'Lanchester' submachine gun, a near perfect copy of the German MP-28.
Canadian Machine Carbine Mk 1
In further cooperation with the Small Arms Ltd at Long Branch, the Canadian military has officially adopted a new compliment and perhaps successor to the Owen. The Machine Carbine Mk 1 features a selective fire trigger with an innovative horizontal magazine to withstand dirt, dust, and sand. Pressure on the top of the trigger would fire the weapon in fully automatic, while pressure on the bottom would fire semi-automatic. While the new machine carbine features a lower rate of fire than the Owen, its reliability and ability to deal selective fire make it quite the potential successor. Many quantities found their way into the arsenal of the Dominions.

Ruskin Rhino
Rhino
With the success of the Rover under its belt, the Ruskin Motor Bodies company designed the Rhino in response to an Australasian government request for a substantially heavier armoured car built upon the CMP chassis. Though substantial changes were made to that CMP chassis, after much work the Rhino was born, and with substantial interchangeability of parts with other CMP-based designs to boot.
Ruskin Hippo
Hippo
To accommodate a substantially larger gun, the Rhino's coaxial machine had to be removed and its turret slightly enlarged. The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

Springfield Model 1903
M1903 Springfield
The Model 1903 was developed at Springfield Arsenal when, following the Spanish-American war, the United States deemed the Krag-Jørgensen rifle inadequate on account of its weak action; at its core, the 1903 was an attempt to combine beneficial features of both the Krag and captureds Spanish Mausers. The rifle was gradually adopted along with the .30-06 cartridge both before and while the rest of the world waged the Weltkrieg.

Type 46/66 Siamese Mauser
Type 46/66
Basic infantryman equipment developed before and during the Weltkrieg. The Type 46/66 is a unique rifle, chambered in the Siamese 8x52mm Rimmed Cartridge, which incorporates what is essentially a hybrid Mauser 96 & 98 action into a body inspired by both Mauser and Arisaka designs. The Type 46 was the primary armament of the Siamese Expeditionary Force during the Weltkrieg. It has become ubiquitous within the armed forces of newly-independent Cambodia.
Type 66 Siamese Mauser
Type 66
Personal and crew-served weapons for infantry as well as the various other bits of kit a soldier needs. While strictly speaking, not truly a Mauser, the Type 66 is an upgraded Japanese export Type 38 Rifle with an action similar to that found on the older Siamese Type 46. It is chambered for the Siamese 8x52mm Rimmed Cartridge. Like its older cousin, upon independence, Cambodia came into possession of a large number of these rifles.

Rifle, Long, Ross Mk III
Ross Mk III
Initially adopted in 1903, the Ross rifle saw several upgrades before the Great War, culminating in the Mk III in 1910. Initially a symbol of patriotism among Canadians as the British refused to license the Lee-Enfield to them, the rifle received an abysmal reputation within the first year of the war, unable to handle the lower quality of British-made ammunition and significantly worse with trench conditions than its contemporaries, and was officially pulled out of service in 1916. Nevertheless, the SMLE was never produced in Canada before the British Revolution, prompting its unwanted return to service as a stopgap until production of the SMLE could begin at Long Branch, Ontario.
Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the loyal Dominions only had access to one arsenal capable of producing its service rifle. Some British engineers from Enfield took refuge in Ontario. With the help of the Lithgow factory in Australia, they continued their work on upgrading the venerable SMLE, resulting in the Mk V. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the receiver instead of the middle, improving sighting radius, accuracy, and speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.

Carbine, Machine, Thompson Mk I
The British Army became vaguely interested in the American submachine guns in around 1925 when Auto-Ordnance attempted to secure a licensing deal with Birmingham Small Arms. The British Revolution sabotaged the deal, but interest was picked back up by the Canadian Army, which secured the licensing of the M1926 to produce at home, albeit in small numbers until the outbreak of civil war in the United States.

Carbine, Commando, De Lisle Mk I
What began as a private venture by engineer William Godfray de Lisle soon caught the attention of the RCMP's Special Branch looking to equip agents with improved weaponry. The De Lisle carbine is, essentially, a shortened SMLE converted to .45 ACP, a subsonic cartridge with an integrated suppressor. The suppressor and ammunition together produced as little as 85.5 decibels when firing, making it very suitable for commando operations.

Though substantial changes were made to that CMP chassis, after much work the Rhino was born, and with substantial interchangeability of parts with other CMP-based designs to boot.
Ruskin Hippo
Hippo
To accommodate a substantially larger gun, the Rhino's coaxial machine had to be removed and its turret slightly enlarged. The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

Mauser Modelo 1895 Rifle Corto
Mauser M1895
The Short Mauser Model 1895 is an upgraded Spanish Mauser in a configuration halfway between a full rifle and a carbine. Throughout Central America, all common variants are chambered in 7x57mm Mauser.
Remington Modelo 1934
Remington M34
The Remington Model 1934 is a bolt-action carbine purpose built for the Central and South American market. It is an upgraded Enfield 1917 incorporating design features from both the Springfield 1903 and Remington 30 sporting rifle, and is chambered in 7x57mm Mauser.
Thompson Modelo 1928A1

Fusil Mauser Modelo 1895
Modelo 1895
In response to an aggressive Argentinian armament programme, Chile adopted a modified Mauser 1893 (commonly known as the Spanish Mauser) chambered in 7x57mm Mauser, and contracted the German company DWM (Deutsche Waffen und Munitionsfabriken) for more than 100,000 rifles and carbines on the pattern. The Chilean Mauser 1895 has earned a reputation for reliability under even the harshest conditions, one which it maintains decades after its adoption.
Carabina Steyr Modelo 1912/35
Modelo 1912/35
Shortly before the Weltkrieg, Chile contracted the Austrian arms manufacturer Steyr for a new Carbine variant of the German Mauser 1898 chambered in 7x57mm like older Chilean rifles. Many of these carbines eventually fell into the hands of the Chilean Carabinieros, who found the rifle unsatisfactory and lobbied for its replacement. In 1935, a new pattern with improved ergonomics and a new larger magazine with more robust feed lips and loader was approved and entered into production shortly thereafter.

MinGan Type 88
MinGan Type 88
Originally a product of late Guangxu era 'Self Strengthening' efforts, the Type 88 was designed as a close copy of the German Gewehr 88 with export modifications. While collectively inferior to newer rifles despite improvements, and often poorly maintained, the weapon is known to be rugged and reliable if unwieldy.\n\nThis variety is produced in the small workshops of the MinGan Insurgent Zone, though equipment shortages and personal preference often see it used alongside a wide variety of other weapons such as the Daodao.
Type 24
Huadong Type 24
This rifle was put into Qing service in 1935 through German assistance after the Restoration. The weapon is functionally a near copy of the German Mauser M1924, and a considerable improvement over the much older Type 88 in terms of weight and recoil.\n\nFirst produced at the Hanyang arsenal with German tools, manufacturing methods have already spread to KMT-held areas, though it may not fully replace its predecessors for some time.
MAS-38
MAS-38
Following the end of the Weltkrieg, a small arms development programme took place in the Service Technique de l'Armement. With the help of MAS (the weapon's industry, the MAS 38 would be France's first submachine gun. An extremely high quality weapon, the MAS 38 features a fire rate of 650 rounds per minute although its appearance is rather odd given its unorthodox layout.\n\nWith the Internationale's support for the revolution in China, thousands of copies have arrived to the Nationalist Government where it has quickly become a favourite for fighting in high-density quarters.
Zhongshan Xinhai Type 30
Zhongshan Type 30
The Type 30 is a semi-automatic rifle based on the General Liu rifle from 1914. It is named the Type 30 rifle in honor of the 30th year of the Republic of China. While appearing similar, the Type 30 is shorter, has the rear of the receiver covered, and the gas-trap system replaced by a simple gas port. The new rifle is also no longer able to switch to manual action and uses magazines from the Bohemian ZH-29 rifle and ZB-26 light machine gun.
RSC 1938 Carbine
The RSC (Fusil Automatique Modele) is a semi-automatic rifle. While earlier varients were long and limited mobility, the carbine version allows for greater movement on the battlefield. This 1938 version features numerous changes to its predecessors such as simpler production, takedown, and cleaning of the rifle.\n\nImported from France, this semi-automatic rifle has been proven to be popular amongst NRA infantrymen.
MAS-36 LG39
This variant of the MAS-36 comes equipped with a folding hollow aluminium stock and strengthened barrel. It can also be used for grenade launching purposes as it comes with a 48mm grenade launcher.
CEAM Modele 1944
A secret design copied from the German line of assault rifles, the CEAM Modele 1944 is one of the Internationale's first assault rifle. Chambered in 0.30 carbine rounds, the rifle even comes with a bipod and a folding buttstock.\n\nAs a result of socialist cooperation between China and the West, a number of CEAM M.1944 rifles have arrived in China.
FM Chauchat & Brandt Mle 27/31
Tried and tested in the trenches of the Weltkrieg, the Chauchat has long been hated by the French infantryman. However, despite the hatred for this weapon, when it is not jammed with dirt and mud, the gun is quite reliable to control, and the Chauchat can be fired from the hip as well. The Brandt mortar is a relic from the Weltkrieg, a refinement of the British 'Stokes' trench mortar. A simple and effective weapon, the mortar can be dissembled into three parts and requires a crew of ten.\n\nDue to French support, a surplus of Chauchats have arrived to assist the NRA.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.\n\nThanks to British aid, a surplus of Lewis light machine guns have arrived in China where it is quite popular amongst NRA infantrymen.
Chatelleraut FM Mle 24/29 & Brandt Mle 35/38
While the Chatelleraut features a low fire rate, it is a robust and reliable weapon; well-liked by French troops for its consistency and weight. The Brandt Mle 35 is a 60mm mortar to be used by infantry companies for indirect fire. Simple and effective, it only requires a crew of five to operate. As most weapons continue to be imported from foreign nations, the Chatelleraut and Brandt Mle 35/38 represent some of the newer models of warfare that are being tried and tested on European battlefields and the American Civil War.
Enfield-Automatic Mk IIIB & ML 3 inch mortar
The Enfield-Automatic Mk IIIB is another creation of the RSAF, who were seeking a replacement for the Vickers and Lewis. The Enfield-Automatic is magazine fed, which reduces its rate of fire but prevents overheating. The resulting weapon is much lighter than belt fed machine guns. The Enfield Automatic is imported from the Union of Britain to the NRA. The ML 3-inch mortar is able to provide effective close artillery support while being portable.
Boys Anti-Tank Rifle
Often nicknamed the 'Elephant Gun' due to its size and large bore, the Boys Anti-Tank rifle can fire a 60g AP projectile at 747 m/s. However, despite the weapon's recoil slide and rubber cushioned buttpad, the recoil of the weapon was said to be painful, frequently causing neck strains and bruised shoulders.
Anti-Armoured Device MK2
The Anti-Armoured Device MK2 is one of the newest pieces of kit developed by Station IX. With an effective firing range of 115 yards, the MK2 will be able to knock out and pierce the heaviest of the internationale's enemy tanks. With the growing mechanisation of war in Asia, the NRA has imported several designs of the British MK2 to improve its conventional anti-tank capabilities.
Cat's Eye
Darkness has always been one of humankind's greatest enemies. The latest developments in Station IX have changed this by creating a top-secret infrared night vision scope. Nicknamed the
