Shanghai Type 1929
Type 1929
'1929' isn't the exact code name, but rather the production date of these armoured cars. In fact, they do not have any official code name at all. The vehicle is equipped with two 30-festival machine guns and six shooting holes for the crew.\n\nIn order to reverse the situation of the Northern Expedition, the Kuomintang commissioned the arsenal in Shanghai concession in March 1928 to manufacture these armoured vehicles. Unfortunately, when these armoured vehicles left the arsenal, the KMT had been completely defeated. Therefore only three original vehicles were ever produced.
Shanghai Type 1930
Type 1930
Similar to its predecessors, the Shanghai Type 1930 was also ordered by the KMT Central Committee from factories in the Shanghai concession. Two prototypes were first built, which had no actual combat record.\n\nThe armoured vehicle weighs five tons, and bears a rotatable turret with a heavy machine gun and a light machine gun.
Shanghai Xinhai Type 36 'Fukong Baolei'
Type 36 'Fukong Baolei'
As an armoured vehicle independently designed by the Kuomintang army, the Xinhai Type 36 armoured car embodies the peak of the development of the Kuomintang armoured vehicle.\n\nThe armoured vehicle has ten wheels and can adapt to a variety of terrain. Due to its external armour, its wheels are mostly concealed, which can make this car appear to float. Therefore, it earned the nickname 'Fukong Baolei' or 'Floating Fortress'.
Type Xinzhong 'New China'
Xinzhong
Through the efforts of a group of Chinese engineers headed by Zhi Bingyuan, combined with the many advantages of German-made trucks, the Kuomintang built the Type Xinzhong with domestically produced parts in the MinGan base at Longyan. Its gearbox, transmission, and steering mechanism are all made in China and can adapt to China's mountainous terrain. Its designer Zhi Bingyuan named it 'Xinzhong', which means 'New China'.
Renault UE Chenillette
Renault UE
During his exile in France, Dai Anlan of the National Revolutionary Army learned some theories about mechanised warfare from the French. When he returned to China, at his request, the French handed him a batch of retired Renault UE along with the design drawings. Dai Anlan believed that he could use this equipment to form China's first mechanised force.

Kratka strojnica Model 1934
Steyr-Solothurn M.34
While a German design by Louis Stange, the  MP 34 was mainly produced by Steyr and was adopted by the Austro-Hungarian Army. As common with early submachine guns, this design was heavy, expensive, but reliable, with a horizontal magazine using the potent 9x25mm Mauser cartridge.
Kratka strojnica Model 38
Zbrojovka Brno M.38
Seeing the need for a submachine-gun using the standard pistol cartridge of the Army, the Monarchy would adopt the ZK 383, designed by brothers Josef and František Koucký at Zbrojovka Brno, as the MP 38. Using a horizontal magazine like the MP 34, the ZK 383 is known for having a light bipod, allowing it to be used as a substitute squad automatic weapon. Later models were heavily simplified for cheaper and faster production, along with a vertical foldable magazine like the Danuvia SMGs.
Kratka strojnica Model 1943
Danuvia M.43
While the Danuvia 39M proved successful, its folding magazine was not enough to compensate for its bulky design for vehicle crewmen. The 43M redesign with a folding metal stock and a new, more reliable foldable magazine design allowed it to be much more compact during transport or storage, without sacrificing firepower.

VsG M.37
To observers and theorists, the civil war in America showcased modern mechanised warfare and showed how much tracked vehicles had improved in reliability and cross-country capabilities. As a stop-gap measure to improve the mobility of its motorised infantry, the Armee-Oberkommando assigned numerous artillery tractors, mostly of the Praga T-series, to its rapid divisions, redesignating them as tracked troop transports until proper armoured carriers could be introduced.
Vozilo sa gusjenicama Model 1938
VsG M.38
The Saurer RR-7 was a hybrid armoured vehicle that could use both tracks or wheels. It was introduced as an artillery tractor in 1937, but proved to be a far more versatile vehicle with its light armour and high mobility. Not only would it fill the role of a reconnaissance vehicle, but also that of a troop transport, making it the Monarchy's first armoured vehicle of that type.
Oklopni transporter Model 1943
OT M.43
With a large number of Nimród self-propelled anti-air vehicles in inventory and its autocannon struggling to fill its role, a large number of them were rebuilt without a turret to fill the role of an armoured ambulance and troop carrier. These made them one of the first modern armoured personnel carriers and significantly lengthening the service life of its parent design.

Springfield Arsenal, Calibre .30-06, Model 1903
SA-1903 Rifle
The Model 1903 was developed at Springfield Arsenal when, following the Spanish-American war, the United States deemed the Krag-Jørgensen rifle inadequate on account of its weak action; at its core, the 1903 was an attempt to combine beneficial features of both the Krag and captureds Spanish Mausers. Beginning just before the Weltkrieg, the rifle was gradually adopted along with the .30-06 cartridge, a process that would take several years.
Springfield Arsenal, Rifle, Calibre .30-06, Model 1903A1
SA-1903A1 Rifle
The M1903A1 Springfield was adopted by the US Army in 1929 as the 'U.S. Rifle, Calibre .30-06, M1903A1'. It incorporated a pistol-grip, chequered buttplate, serrated trigger, and several smaller improvements into the original 1903 design.
Auto-Ordnance, Rifle, Calibre .30-06, Model 2
AO-2 Rifle
The AO-2 originated as the Thompson Auto-Rifle and used so-called 'Blish Lock'. An abject failure in the U.S. Army's 1929 self-loading rifle trials, the design was taken up again after the outbreak of the American Civil War. Due to the WCA's need to develop the rifle as quickly as possible, interchangeability of parts and ease of construction were important to the design process. Though some claim the AO-2 is a less effective rifle than the Johnson adopted by Federal forces, it could be mass produced with ease.
Auto-Ordnance, Carbine, Calibre .30, Model 3
AO-3 Carbine
The AO-3 Carbine's development began shortly after the end of Civil War. The AO-3 is little more than a carbine version of the early AO-2 rifle, with the addition of a three-position fire selector.
Auto-Ordnance, Submachine Gun, Calibre .45, Model 1928A1

Uhl, Rocket Launcher, 2.36 inch, Model 2
U-2 'Stovepipe'
The U-2 is man-portable recoilless anti-tank rocket launcher created by Continental Army captain Edward Uhl. The weapon uses 2.36 inch self-propelled rockets and has an effective range of 140 metres.
Uhl, Rocket Launcher, 2.36 inch, Model 5
U-5 'Bazooka'
The U-5 is improved version of the U-2. A trigger operated magneto replaced the U-2's battery ignition system and a safety was added. The tube could be broken down for easier carrying, an important consideration for infantry. New, more reliable rockets were also introduced.

Continental Army, Infrared Sniperscope, Model 120
CA-120 Sniperscope
Developed specifically for the AO-4 Carbine, which had special mountings on its receiver for various models of infra-red night-sighting devices, the CA-120 is the first infrared sniperscope adopted for general use by the Continental Army. While the CA-120 has its flaws, like a poor range and an easily damaged Infrared emitter, the CA-120 still has tremendous value when used correctly.
Continental Army, Infrared Sniperscope, Model 123
CA-123 Sniperscope
In response to the poor reception of the CA-120 Sniperscope, the Sniperscope CA-123 was adopted. With almost twice the effective range of the CA-120, a more robust infrared light source, and the T23 flash hider, the CA-123 is a vast improvement over its predecessor, though some soldiers complain of the light source bleeding into the visible spectrum and thus giving away their position.

Dodge, 1937, 2 1/2 tons
DMJ
The DMJ is a classic army-standard 6x6 2 1/2 tons truck with high cross-country capability. Developed in 1937 at the request of the U.S. Army, with the start of the American Civil War, Dodge facilities were seized by the WCA and production of the truck began for the Continental Army.
White Motor, Half-Track Car, Carrier, Model 6
WM-6 Half-Track Car
The WM-6 is an armoured half-track design inspired by French half tracks. Developed by the newly seized White Motors, the WM-6 is multi-purpose truck which can serve as a troop carrier, artillery tractor, or reconnaissance vehicle.
Continental Army, Half-Track, Carrier, Model 9
CA-9 Half-Track
The CA-9 is a development on the WM-6 designed internally by the Continental Army. The new half-track is a larger version of the WM-6 equipped with two M-3 M1919 machine guns and a B2 machine gun, as well as a rear door and five additional seats in the rear.
Continental Army, Half-Track, Carrier, Model 11
CA-11 Half-Track
The CA-10 was developed concurrently with the CA-9 as a heavier alternative to that vehicle. Improved after the deployment of the CA-9 and marked as the CA-11, the vehicle is both easier to produce and more heavily armoured, but lacks speed, much to the chagrin of former U.S. officers in the Continental Army.
Dodge, 1937, 2 1/2 tons, CA-28
DMJ 'Xylophone'
A DMJ truck with a mounted CA-28 rocket launcher, the vehicle is commonly known as the 'Xylophone'. While individual launchers are known to be a bit inaccurate, the volume of fire this design can output is impressive.
Dodge, 1937, 2 1/2 tons, CA-28
DMJ 'Xylophone'
A DMJ truck with a mounted CA-28 rocket launcher, the vehicle is commonly known as the 'Xylophone'. While individual launchers are known to be a bit inaccurate, the volume of fire this design can output is impressive.
Roebling-Dodge, Landing Vehicle, Tracked, Model 2
RD-2 LVT
The Landing Vehicle series of amphibious warfare vehicle were originally intended to serve solely as cargo carrier for ship to shore operations. They, of course, evolved into highly capable assault troop and fire support vehicles. Developed by Donald Roebling in 1935, the civilian rescue vehicle 
Roebling-Dodge, Landing Vehicle, Tracked, Model 4
RD-4 LVT
The RD-4 improved upon the RD-2 with greatly increased carrying capacity, albeit at the expense of speed despite improvements to its engine. Unlike its predecessor, it also mounted either a heavy machine gun or a flame-thrower.
White Motor, Scout Car, Model 2
WM-2 Scout Car
The WM-2 Scout Car is a lightly armoured, open topped, and machine gun armed four wheel drive vehicle developed for a reconnaissance role. A further development of the Scout Car concept, which was intended to replace cavalry in reconnaissance, the M2 Scout Car finished trials at the end of 1935, and amidst increasing tensions in the U.S. was accepted into Army service despite its flaws. In WCA service, the vehicle was known as the WM-2.
White Motor, Scout Car, Model 4
WM-4 Scout Car
The WM-4 Scout Car is a development of the WM-2 designed with experience gained during the Civil War in mind. The WM-4 sports several small improves born of wartime experience, such as an armoured fender and front roller, and a more reliable engine. Although the Scout Car concept itself has received criticism for its focus on mobility over survivability, the WM-4 was nonetheless accepted into service.
Marmon-Herrington, Armoured Car, Model 6
MH-6 Armoured Car
The MH-6 Armoured Car was developed at the end of American Civil War. Known first as the DHT-5, the vehicle proved highly unpopular and was heavily redesigned to fit the Army's requirements. Introduced again as the DHT-6 in 1942, the new car was unable to penetrate thick armoured vehicles with its 37mm gun. It did, however, serve successfully in reconnaissance and infantry support roles under the name MH-6.
Continental Army, Armoured Car, Model 10
CA-10 Armoured Car
The CA-10 Armoured Car is an MH-6 variant with a 57-mm gun to provide proper anti-tank support. Only minor changes were made to the MH-6 to accomidate the new gun.
Rock Island Arsenal, Field Gun, 75-mm, Model 4
RIA-4 75-mm Gun
Rock Island Arsenal, Howitzer, Towed, 105-mm, Model 6
RIA-6 105-mm Howitzer

Springfield Model 1903
M1903 Springfield
The Model 1903 was developed at Springfield Arsenal when, following the Spanish-American war, the United States deemed the Krag-Jørgensen rifle inadequate on account of its weak action; at its core, the 1903 was an attempt to combine beneficial features of both the Krag and captured Spanish Mausers. The rifle was gradually adopted along with the .30-06 cartridge both before and while the rest of the world waged the Weltkrieg.
Remington Modelo 1934
Remington M34
The Remington Model 1934 is a bolt-action carbine purpose built for the Central and South American market. It is an upgraded Enfield 1917 incorporating design features from both the Springfield 1903 and Remington 30 sporting rifle, and is chambered in 7x57mm Mauser.
Thompson Model 1928A1
Thompson M1928A1
Kiraly-Cristóbal Carbine
Cristóbal Carbine
While Hungarian arms designer Pál Király's heavy submachine guns were adopted by the Dual Monarchy, they nonetheless fell short of Király's own expectations. The Cristóbal is a development of those SMGs redesigned around a new intermediate calibre cartride. While it was ultimately rejected by the Dual Monarchy, the Dominican Republic, where Király had once considered immigrating, proved more receptive, adopting the gun as the 'Cristóbal' Carbine after the city of San Cristóbal. Cuba licensed production soon after its Dominican introduction.

Mauser G98 M1903
Mauser G98
Mauser G98 was the main service rifle of the Ottoman Army during the Weltkrieg. The Ottoman M1903, chambered in 7.65mm, was the most common variant of Mauser in use. Pronounced 'Mavzer' by the Ottomans, this iconic rifle achieved great fame and became deeply ingrained in the Turkish subconscious.\n'One side of me withered from sorrows / the other from a Mavzer wound'
Ankara ATF M38
ATF M38
Ankara ATF M38 was the name given to the Ottoman modification of Mauser Gewehr 98. Starting in 1933, the Ottoman Empire updated their old rifles to a common configuration known as the Model of 1938 and all in 8x57 Mauser cartridge. The standardisation of all Mausers in Ottoman inventory helped ease the logistical nightmare of having to supply 4 different types of rifle ammunition to all units.
Kırıkkale T-60
T-60
Kırıkkale T-60 Semi Automatic Rifle was based on the receiver of the Gewehr 41, the T-60 is issued with a lighter build and shortened stock, emphasising reliability and speed over range and accuracy. This rifle saw wide use across all branches of service in the Turkish Military.

Kulometná pistole vzor 1934
Steyr-Solothurn vz. 34
While a German design by Louis Stange, the  MP 34 was mainly produced by Steyr and was adopted by the Austro-Hungarian Army. As common with early submachine guns, this design was heavy, expensive, but reliable, with a horizontal magazine using the potent 9x25mm Mauser cartridge.
Kulometná pistole vzor 38
Zbrojovka Brno vz. 38
Seeing the need for a submachine-gun using the standard pistol cartridge of the Army, the Monarchy would adopt the ZK 383, designed by brothers Josef and František Koucký at Zbrojovka Brno, as the MP 38. Using a horizontal magazine like the MP 34, the ZK 383 is known for having a light bipod, allowing it to be used as a substitute squad automatic weapon. Later models were heavily simplified for cheaper and faster production, along with a vertical foldable magazine like the Danuvia SMGs.

Pásové vozidlo vzor 1937
PV vz. 37
To observers and theorists, the civil war in America showcased modern mechanised warfare and showed how much tracked vehicles had improved in reliability and cross-country capabilities. As a stop-gap measure to improve the mobility of its motorised infantry, the Armee-Oberkommando assigned numerous artillery tractors, mostly of the Praga T-series, to its rapid divisions, redesignating them as tracked troop transports until proper armoured carriers could be introduced.
Pásové vozidlo vzor 1938
PV vz. 38
The Saurer RR-7 was a hybrid armoured vehicle that could use both tracks or wheels. It was introduced as an artillery tractor in 1937, but proved to be a far more versatile vehicle with its light armour and high mobility. Not only would it fill the role of a reconnaissance vehicle, but also that of a troop transport, making it the Monarchy's first armoured vehicle of that type.
Obrněný transportér vzor 1943
OT vz. 43
With a large number of Nimród self-propelled anti-air vehicles in inventory and its autocannon struggling to fill its role, a large number of them were rebuilt without a turret to fill the role of an armoured ambulance and troop carrier. These made them one of the first modern armoured personnel carriers and significantly lengthening the service life of its parent design.

Ishapore SMLE Mk III
Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
The Ishapore Rifle Factory was founded in 1904 and began producing the venerable Lee-Enfield (SMLE) rifle that would serve throughout the Great War. However, the loss of much of British India after the British Revolution also meant the loss of the Ishapore factory, one of the only two factories producing the SMLE outside of Britain, putting a serious strain in rifle production for the remnants of the Empire for years until what was salvaged from Ishapore could be set up in friendly territory.
SMLE Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the need to rearm what remained of the Empire became paramount. British engineers from the RSAF (Royal Small Arms Factory) began taking refuge in Ontario to work on an existing Lee-Enfield. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the side instead of the middle, which features improved sighting radius, sighting accuracy, and sighting speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.
Owen Mk 1
Owen Machine Carbine
With the prospect of war coming, the Army has requested a submachine gun to be developed for close quarters and urban warfare, especially with the destructiveness that characterises the civil war in America. The Canadian Army officially adopted a weapon designed by young Australian private Evelyn Owen. The 
Long Branch Mk 1
SLR Mk 1
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat. Many copies were sent to arm the Dominions of the Empire in preparation for the eventual homecoming.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-tank No. 68
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I (PIAT) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the Canadian Army during the Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the Canadian Army has recommissioned them back into service.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Vickers M Mk II & ML 3 inch mortar
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG features a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It has been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar is a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it is reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Vickers M Mk III & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar is a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.
Tabby Scope
The Tabby Scope is one of the Canadian government's most closesly guarded secrets; the process of development is even hidden from the fellow members of the Entente. A chance discovery by scientists in the ISAC, the scope is shaped in the form of a canteen but contains a monocular infrared scope along with a battery. Assigned to only the most elite of the elite, the scope allows the unprecedented advantage of having vision in the dark.
BSA Thompson M1926
The Canadian Army has formally adopted the iconic American submachine gun associated with crime and vice. The M1928A1 is the service variant of the classic M1921 Thompson Machine gun, and although production is expensive, the gun is well-liked among the troops in the field who praise its efficiency and reliability. While most variants rely on smaller 'box' magazines, some other variants feature large and bulky fifty-round drum magazines.
Lanchester Submachine Gun
With the outbreak of all out war in the United States and the looming clouds of war in Europe, the Canadian Army desperately needs arms for the homecoming. With little time to prepare and research, the Canadian Army has commissioned the 'Lanchester' submachine gun, a near perfect copy of the German MP-28.
Canadian Machine Carbine Mk 1
In further cooperation with the Small Arms Ltd at Long Branch, the Canadian military has officially adopted a new compliment and perhaps successor to the Owen. The Machine Carbine Mk 1 features a selective fire trigger with an innovative horizontal magazine to withstand dirt, dust, and sand. Pressure on the top of the trigger would fire the weapon in fully automatic, while pressure on the bottom would fire semi-automatic. While the new machine carbine features a lower rate of fire than the Owen, its reliability and ability to deal selective fire make it quite the potential successor. Many quantities found their way into the arsenal of the Dominions.

Ruskin Rhino
Rhino
With the success of the Rover under its belt, the Ruskin Motor Bodies company designed the Rhino in response to an Australasian government request for a substantially heavier armoured car built upon the CMP chassis. Though substantial changes were made to that CMP chassis, after much work the Rhino was born, and with substantial interchangeability of parts with other CMP-based designs to boot.

Hippo

The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

Braat Overvalwagen
Overvalwagen
Designed in 1940 and based on a Chevrolet COE (Cab Over Chassis) truck. In order to deflect or stop small arms fire, the sloped hull was 20 mm thick at the front glacis and 12 mm thick at the sides and rear. The main cab counted one driver armoured hinged panel and a side vision slit, while the commander position received a three-faced casemate housing an air-cooled cal.30 (7.62 mm) Browning machine gun or a liquid-cooled .303 (7.7 mm) Vickers and a rear hatch.

Krag-Jørgensen Gevær M/89
Gevær M/89
The Gevær M/89 has been the primary Danish rifle since 1889. Considered to be on par with the rest of the rifles in Europe, the gun has a length of 133 cm, making it dangerous in the hands of a marksman. However, the gun's length sacrifices its mobility, and it can be pretty heavy and less manageable in close-range fighting. Thus, some variants of the weapon were modified into carbines for shorter engagements.
Krag-Jørgensen Fodfolkskarabin M/89-24
Fodfolkskarabin M/89-24
The Danish variation of the Krag-Jørgensen features a forward-hinged magazine door, rimmed ammunition, and outer steel liner for the barrel. Chambered for an 8x58R cartridge, the Danish rifle was often placed in the hands of the reserves as an infantry carbine. As Scandanavia finds itself increasingly on the frontline of a potential European war, the Danish Army has pushed for more 89/24s for frontline military service.

Lettet-Forsøgs M/31
A submachine gun of Danish origin, the \
Fabrique Nationale Model 1943
FN-43
A semi-automatic rifle of Belgian origin, the Fabrique Nationale Model 1943, was shipped to various countries in Europe. As the Danish military command desired a semi-automatic battle rifle to improve the efficiency and firepower of the Danish riflemen, many orders were placed on the FN-1943. Firing in 7.92x57mm Mauser, the gun is gas operated, and initial testing has revealed that the weapon competes well against European and American models.

Springfield Krag-Jørgensen Modelo 1898
Springfield M1898
The Krag-Jørgensen rifle was adopted by the United States in 1892 to replace the ageing Springfield Model 1884. While the Krags proved superior at close ranges to comparable Mauser designs in the Spanish-American war, the rifle's flimsy action in comparison to newer bolt designs necessitated a replacement a mere decade after its adoption. During the American occupations of several Central American nations in the early 20th century, many stockpiled Krag rifles, mostly of the 1898 rifle pattern, were given to local forces along with droves of surplus ammunition.

Kiraly-Cristóbal Carbine
Cristóbal Carbine
While Hungarian arms designer Pál Király's heavy submachine guns were adopted by the Dual Monarchy, they nonetheless fell short of Király's own expectations. The Cristóbal is a development of those SMGs redesigned around a new intermediate calibre cartride. While it was ultimately rejected by the Dual Monarchy, the Dominican Republic, where Király had once considered immigrating, proved more receptive, adopting the gun as the 'Cristóbal' Carbine after the city of San Cristóbal.

Selbstladegewehr 40
Selbstlader 40
Designed by the Mauser company in response to a German Army desire for a self-loading rifle with a backup bolt action, the Selbstladegewehr 40 was a massive leap forward in German small arms design. While initial prototypes were highly inaccurate, heavy, and prone to jamming, the German army eventually dropped some its other specific requirements for the rifle, and the end result, the predecessor the rifle that would ultimately be adopted, proved effective.

Remington M34

Rifle No.1 Mk III
No.1 Mk III
The Mk III has served the Tommy since 1907, an iconic weapon of the Weltkrieg. The SMLE Mk III came with the 1907 Pattern bayonet and a simplified rear sight arrangement, and a fixed charger guide. During the Weltkrieg, demand outstripped supply and thus many copies of the Mk III comes with the deletion of the magazine cutoff.
Rifle No.4 Mk I
No.4 Mk I
After the revolution and civil war it became apparent that the No.3 Enfield was not fit for urban combat. Trials for an entirely new rifle were finally held in 1932, but would largely fail to bear fruit, with new designs unable to match the old Enfield or receiving unfavourable troop evaluations. In light of this showing, Enfield replacement efforts were ultimately shelved. In 1935, however, a joint effort between The Revolutionary Small Arms factory in Enfield and Birmingham Small Arms was presented to the government. The new rifle was a development on the old Lee-Enfield with a shorter barrel, side mounted sling, and a simplified bolt system. While similar to its predecessors, the No.4 is stronger and easier to produce, though a bit heavier due to its barrel. An interesting fact about the No.4 is that it comes with an aperture sight that is set at increments of 100 yds (91m); calibrated for 200-1,300 yards (183-1189 metres).
No.2 Machine-Carbine Mk I
No.2 MC Mk I
With the future of warfare gravitating towards hand-held automatic weapons and the clouds of war looming in Europe once more, the technicians at the RSAF and the men at Station IX have created a new submachine gun. Light and portable, the No.2 machine carbine is ideally suited for urban operations and medium to close-range combat.
Rifle No.5 Mk I
No.5 Mk I
The No.5 is issued as an improved successor to the No.4, adopting refined ergonomics, a slightly lighter stock and redesigned sights. It delivers equal or better accuracy at combat ranges, eases handling on long marches and simplifies maintenance and production without compromising the No.4's reliability and rapid-fire characteristics.
Boys Anti-Tank Rifle
Often nicknamed the 'Elephant Gun' due to its size and large bore, the Boys Anti-Tank rifle can fire a 60g AP projectile at 747 m/s. However, despite the weapon's recoil slide and rubber cushioned buttpad, the recoil of the weapon was said to be painful, frequently causing neck strains and bruised shoulders.
Anti-Armoured Device MK2
The Anti-Armoured Device MK2 is one of the newest pieces of kit developed by Station IX. With an effective firing range of 115 yards, the MK2 will be able to knock out and pierce the heaviest of the Kaiser's panzers.
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The Vickers MG and Stokes mortar were iconic weapons of the British Army during the Weltkrieg. Following the revolution and the civil war; large quantities of these weapons still exist in the arsenals of the RSAF. Though outclassed in this day and age; they can still be used effectively.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Enfield-Automatic Mk IIIB & ML 3 inch mortar
The Enfield-Automatic Mk IIIB is another creation of the RSAF, who were seeking a replacement for the Vickers and Lewis. The Enfield-Automatic is magazine fed, which reduces its rate of fire but prevents overheating. The resulting weapon is much lighter than belt fed machine guns, and will serve the next generation of Tommies in the trenches. The ML 3inch mortar is able to provide effective close artillery support while being portable.
Enfield-Automatic Mk V & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
As the war drags on, improvements to existing models are constantly being made. The Mk V variant of the Enfield-Automatic features a telescopic sight and an improved cooling system. The ML 4.2 inch mortar is a heavy mortar. Quite heavy but deadly, the mortar requires to be towed in order for it to be brought to the battlefield.
No.1 MC Mk 1
The latest development in British ingenuity, the Mark 1 Machine-Carbine is a fully automatic sub-machine gun. A successful and reliable design; the Mark 1 features a steady rate of fire and is quite light, weighing at about 6 pounds (2.7 kg), making it perfect for close-quarters combat.

The MK IV variant of the MR Mk 1

Darkness has always been one of humankind's greatest enemies. The latest developments in Station IX have changed this by creating a top-secret infrared night vision scope. Nicknamed the

British Rail Stanier Class 48F
BR Class 48F
After experimenting with the Stanier Class 42MT and producing the successful BR Class 44MT, William Stanier designed the Class 48F solely for heavy freight service. Entering service in 1935, the Class 48F is one of the most successful locomotives of British Rail, seeing widespread service over all regions.
British Rail Riddles Class 90F
BR Class 90F
After Robert Riddle's assignment to the Ministry of Supply, he quickly saw to designing several classes of locomotives that prioritise low cost over service life, able to be quickly produced. The modified Class 48F became the Class 90F, incorporating a number of changes. Extensive modifications were made to the boiler and fireboxes to use copper, along with simplifications to the valve gears. Welding was also implemented gradually into the construction of both the locomotives and tenders. The highly simplified production methods allowed for a large number of BR Class 90F to be built cheaply and rapidly.
British Rail Bulleid Class 135MTA
BR Class 135MTA
Chief Mechanical Engineer of the Southern Region, Oliver Bulleid, believed that normal locomotives operating in the region would be easily taken out by any incoming hostile aircraft. The Class 135MTA would be his answer, a class of air-smoothed 4-6-2 Pacific steam locomotives, designed with redundancies in mind and armoured with two inches of armour plating over the boiler, wheels and cab. The class would be nicknamed ‘Spam Cans' by their crews due to the air smoothed armour casing.
Breech-Loaded 9.2-inch Mk. XIII
BL 9.2-inch
Repurposed by the Republican Army from abandoned and unused Royal Navy 9.2-inch rifles, they were installed onto a makeshift gun carriage and pushed into service as a weapon to assist in warding off a potential invasion.

Mauser Model 1898
Mauser 1898
In response to several commercially successful short rifles produced on the basis of the Mauser 1898 by competing arms manufacturers, the Mauser company developed its owned moderately shortened 1898 rifle. Two related patterns of this design were given the designation 'standard modell' by Mauser. Many small nations, including Ethiopia purchased these new guns in large quantites; the Ethiopian variant is the older of the two and is chambered in 7.65×53mm Mauser. With the revolt, large quantities of these rifles were captured by Eritrea and became the main rifle of its fledgling armed forces.

Mauser Model 1898
Mauser 1898

Hanyang Type 88
Hanyang Type 88
Originally a product of late Guangxu era 'Self Strengthening' efforts, the Type 88 was designed as a close copy of the German Gewehr 88 with export modifications. While collectively inferior to newer rifles despite improvements, and often poorly maintained, the weapon is known to be rugged and reliable if unwieldy.\n\nThis variety is mainly produced at the Xining Arsenal in Qinghai, though equipment shortages and personal preference often see it used alongside non-standard weapons such as the Dadao.
Türk M1887 Mauser Tüfek
Tüfek M1887
The Turkish M1887 Mauser is a further refinement of the M1871/84 German Mauser. The action was strengthened by the addition of a single locking lug and calibre was further reduced. This rfle fired the smallest calibre black poweder lead bullet military catridge adopted by any nation, and was the last black powder catridge rifle manufactured by Mauser.
AVS Type 38
AVS 38
Hanyang Type 35
Hanyang Type 35
The Type 35 is a semi-automatic rifle based on the General Liu rifle from 1914. While appearing similar, the Type 35 is shorter, has the rear of the receiver covered, and the gas-trap system replaced by a simple gas port. The new rifle is also no longer able to switch to manual action and uses magazines from the Bohemian ZH-29 rifle and ZB-26 light machine gun.
Citroën P21 Type C6 'Croisière Jaune'
Citroën P21
In November 1931, the great Croisière Jaune (Yellow Expedition) organised by Citroën arrived in Xinjiang. Yang Zengxin, the governor of Xinjiang, was surprised by the French expedition across the Silk Road, and warmly received the expedition team. Seeing that these trucks can drive continuously in harsh environments, during the expedition's stay in Xinjiang, Yang Zengxin proposed to purchase a batch of Citroën P21 cars, and the expedition readily agreed to this request because the purpose of their expedition was to advertise for Citroën. Soon after the expedition arrived in Beijing in February 1932, the French shipped the first batch of auto parts and engineers to Xinjiang. This truck has also become the most common automobile in Xinjiang.

Kivääri Pelo M/38
Pelo M/38
Though his initial designs were deemed prohibitively expensive by the Finnish Army in the early 1930s, Carl Pelo's long-recoil semi-automatic rifle caught the attention of Finland's neighbour - Sweden. A team of skilled Swedish arms designers ultimately managed to reduce the production costs of the rifle, and it was ultimately adopted by the Swedes despite still being quite complex.\nUpon returning to Finland, Carl Pelo successfully advocated the rifle's adoption in his native Finland, which previously lacked semi-automatic rifles entirely.
Suomi-konepistooli 41
Suomi KP/-41
The KP/-41, while outwardly appearing nearly identical to the older KP/-31, is a vast departure in arms design. The gun began as an experiment by Aimo Lahti in hybridising the 8mm Mauser rifle cartridge with 9mm Parabellum; The new large pistol cartridge born from his experiments, however, became the central focus of subsequent Finnish arms design. The KP/-41 itself began as a derivative of Lahti's own 'Suomi' submachine gun rechambered for his new cartridge, and indeed, while retention of the general shape and feel of the KP/-31 was preferred, ultimately, the new design's massive recoil impetus led to both further external changes to the weapon's body as well as a reduction in its cyclic rate.

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Sd.Kfz. 11
The Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11 was developed from 1934 to 1938 and involved Borgward AG and, from 1937 onwards, the design became associated with Hannoversche Maschinenbau AG. Initially intended to be a prime mover, the Sd.Kfz. 11 soon was transferred to the role of a transporter, when the need for an all-terrain vehicle became apparent. Capable of carrying eight soldiers in addition to towing a gun or a trailer, the truck became the main vehicle of panzergrenadiers.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251
Sd.Kfz. 251
Developed on the chassis of Sd.Kfz. 11, the Schützenpanzerwagen Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251 half-tracks (commonly referred to as \
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 256
Sd.Kfz. 256
In late 1943, the German Army asked Auto-Union to develop a light, fully tracked reconnaissance vehicle, capable of carrying 6-8 men in an open-top superstructure. Introduced in 1944 the Schwerer Schützenpanzerwagen 'Kätzchen' (Heavy Armoured Personnel Carrier 'Kitten') had the hull's shape similar to the hull of the Stu.Pz.Kpfw. VII, but much smaller. The vehicle has front wheel drive with five or six overlapping steel road wheels and was designed to attain speeds in excess of 50 km/hr and has light armour. Warmly met by the German Military despite not very high speed, the vehicle was adopted in service under the name Sd.Kfz. 256.

Fabrique Nationale Mauser Modèle 1935
FN M35
The Fabrique Nationale Model 1935 is a slightly shortned Model 1889 Mauser with a plethora of upgrades including a new bolt and locking system, a more reliable magazine, as well as improved ergonomics. The model made for the Belgian military is chambered in 7.65×53mm Mauser.

Shenyang Type 38
Type 38
With designs and machinery provided by Japan in the early 1920s, the Shenyang Arsenal has produced a steady stream of slightly modified copies of the Arisaka Type 38 Rifle.\n\nBy 1936 over 150,000 have been produced though many units, especially those on the frontier, retain a more eclectic variety of service weapons.
Dashuai Type 24 Rifle
Type 24
The Dashuai Type 24, named for the 'Old Marshal' Zhang Zuolin and numbered according to the Republican calendar, is functionally a copy of the Japanese Arisaka Type 99. Minor modifications have been made to accommodate the cheaper and more common Mauser S84/98 III bayonet, and the weapon has been appreciated by the troops for its increased stopping power and superior build quality.
Kawamura Type 14
Type 14
In 1931, the Japanese Army approached Kijiro Nambu to design a new semi-automatic rifle. His design, based on the Pedersen Rifle, which had been unsuccessfully presented to Japan years earlier, ultimately led nowhere due both to difficulties with the Pedersen delayed-blowback toggle-lock system and the unrealistic goals of the Army. However, two refined rifles based upon the Pedersen system would resurface several years later. The design of Masaya Kawamura, of the Nippon Special Steel Company, would ultimately be chosen and adopted by Japan as the Type 14 due to its reliability and ability to function without the need for wax-lubricated cartridges.

Minsheng
Designed by Daniel Myers, an American engineer, to suit Chinese circumstances, the Minsheng truck is China's first natively produced automobile. Its name, 'People's Livelihood', is derived from the last of Sun Yat-sen's Three Principles, which Zhang Zuolin claims to admire.\n\nUnfortunately the road to mass production has been littered with delays and shortfalls, believed to be the work of Japanese and Qing saboteurs.

Fusil Mauser Modelo 1895
Modelo 1895
In response to an aggressive Argentinian armament programme, Chile adopted a modified Mauser 1893 (commonly known as the Spanish Mauser) chambered in 7x57mm Mauser, and contracted the German company DWM (Deutsche Waffen und Munitionsfabriken) for more than 100,000 rifles and carbines on the pattern. The Chilean Mauser 1895 has earned a reputation for reliability under even the harshest conditions, one which it maintains decades after its adoption. It mainly with these Chilean Mausers that the revolutionary government in Patagonia is equipped.
Carabina Steyr Modelo 1912/35
Modelo 1912/35
Shortly before the Weltkrieg, Chile contracted the Austrian arms manufacturer Steyr for a new Carbine variant of the German Mauser 1898 chambered in 7x57mm like older Chilean rifles. Many of these carbines eventually fell into the hands of the Chilean Carabinieros, who found the rifle unsatisfactory and lobbied for its replacement. In 1935, a new pattern with improved ergonomics and a new larger magazine with more robust feed lips and loader was approved and entered into production shortly thereafter. A few months after its initial production, the rifles began to trickle into Patagonia.
MAS 36
Hafdasa 'Ballester-Rigaud C1' mo
Hafdasa C-4
Hafdasa C-4
The Hafdasa C-4 is a machine pistol of Argentine origin that can be fired semiautomatically or automatically. Originally derived from the HAFDASA 'Ballester Rigaud C1' model rifle, the C-4 variant is particularly heavy, reflecting the weapon's sturdiness.
Halcón M-43
Halcón M-43
A submachine gun of purely Argentine origin, the Halcon M-1943 was developed in 1941 by the Fabrica de Armas Halcon (Falcon Gun Factory) due to a need for submachine guns. Long employed by the Comites Autonomous de Salvacion Publica in Buenos Aires, the military has recently adopted the submachine gun. Military observers worldwide have compared the weapon to the American Thompson submachine gun, praising its reliability and rate of fire.

Fusil Lebel Modèle 1886 M93
Lebel Mle M93
The Lebel is an 8mm bolt action rifle that has entered French military service since 1887. The world's first firearm to utilise smokeless powder, the rifle is known for its durability and long range. The primary weapon of the French soldier in the trenches of the Weltkrieg; large quantities of the rifle still exist even now under the new regime of the Commune.
MAS Modèle 1936
MAS-36
The proximity of France to the destested German Empire has hastened the call for a more modern bolt action rifle. The new MAS 36 rifle has been chosen by the Commune as the new bolt-action rifle for the revolution. A short 'carbine' style rifle that features dirt resistance and is relatively cheap to produce; the MAS 36 shall serve as the new standard arm to defend France from all her enemies.
MAS Modèle 1938
MAS-38
Following the end of the Weltkrieg, a small arms development programme took place in the Service Technique de l'Armement. With the help of MAS (the weapon's industry, the MAS 38 would be France's first submachine gun. An extremely high quality weapon, the MAS 38 features a fire rate of 650 rounds per minute although its appearance is rather odd given its unorthodox layout.
MAS Modèle 1940/42
MAS-40/42
The MAS-40/42 is the latest in a series of Communard semi-automatic rifles. Unlike its predecessors, however, the MAS-40/42 was found to be exceptionally reliable in French military testing. The original five round internal magazine, however, was deemed insufficient and a new variant of the MAS-40/42, the 40/42, was developed featuring a detachable ten round box magazine.
FM Chauchat & Brandt Mle 27/31
Tried and tested in the trenches of the Weltkrieg, the Chauchat has long been hated by the French infantryman. However, despite the hatred for this weapon, when it is not jammed with dirt and mud, the gun is quite reliable to control, and the Chauchat can be fired from the hip as well. The Brandt mortar is a relic from the Weltkrieg, a refinement of the British 'Stokes' trench mortar. A simple and effective weapon, the mortar can be dissembled into three parts and requires a crew of ten.
Hotchkiss M1922 & Brandt Mle 27/31
A sturdy and reliable weapon, the Hotchkiss M1922 is a light machine gun developed by Hotchkiss. Capable of firing 450 rounds per minute, the gun has a regulator that can adjust the rate of fire. The Hotchkiss can be fed from an overhead magazine or 15/24/30 round feed strips.
Chatelleraut FM Mle 24/29 & Brandt Mle 35/38
It is clear to the command of the Commune that the Chauchat and Hotchkiss are seriously outdated. Our designers have come up with a much lighter gun in preparation for the coming war than the Hotchkiss. While the Chatelleraut features a low fire rate, it is a robust and reliable weapon; well-liked by our troops for its consistency and weight. The Brandt Mle 35 is a 60mm mortar to be used by infantry companies for indirect fire. Simple and effective, it only requires a crew of five to operate.
MAS AA-42 & Brandt Mle 1942
Experience in the Second Weltkrieg has led to an interest in general purpose machine guns to serve a variety of tactical roles. With nearly double the rate of fire of the Chatelleraut, the AA-42 is a belt-fed monster that will mow down anything in its path. The Mle 42 is a considerable improvement over its predecessor, with an emphasis on a greater close quarters support weapon.
Canon de 75mm mle 1897 modifee 1933
The beloved French 75mm is a field gun developed for anti personal usage. While maintaing the shield and wheels of the original field gun, this variant has increased elevation and a 58 degree traverse.
LRAC de 73mm Mle 1943
An anti-tank rocket launcher, the LRAC can fire a 73mm high explosive antitank projectile capable of penetrating over 11 inches of rolled homogenous armour. The fear of every German tank crew, the LRAC has a shield to protect the operator from the rocket's back blast.
RSC 1938 Carbine
The RSC (Fusil Automatique Modele) is a semi-automatic rifle. While earlier varients were long and limited mobility, the carbine version allows for greater movement on the battlefield. This 1938 version features numerous changes to its predecessors such as simpler production, takedown, and cleaning of the rifle.
MAS-36 LG39
This variant of the MAS-36 comes equipped with a folding hollow aluminium stock and strengthened barrel. It can also be used for grenade launching purposes as it comes with a 48mm grenade launcher.
CEAM Modele 1944
A secret design copied from the German line of assault rifles, the CEAM Modele 1944 is France's first assault rifle. Chambered in 0.30 carbine rounds, the rifle even comes with a bipod and a folding buttstock.

État 141 b/c
État 141 b/c
A locomotive with a low axle load, the class could operate not only on the former L'État and the L'Ouest railways, but across the entirety of France. Being simple to operate and reliable, the class would be mass produced after the French Civil War by the SNCF, with the ‘B' and ‘C' denoting different boiler pressures and the output power.
SNCF 150P
SNCF 150P
The powerful 2-10-0 locomotives designed for heavy ore and freight trains were soon redesigned to be cheaply produced and able to be widely used across the French railway network.
SNCF 241P
SNCF 241P
A 4-8-2 ‘Mountain' type locomotive originally designed for express passenger service from Paris to Marseille, the high horsepower of the locomotive suited it for being converted into pulling armoured supply trains to the frontlines.

Karabinek wzór 1895/30
Mannlicher wz. 95/30
The Mannlicher Karabinek wzór 1895/1930 (German: Infanterie Repetier-Gewehr M.95) was the service rifle of the Austro-Hungarian armed forces during the Weltkrieg. Designed by Ferdinand von Mannlicher, it used a straight-pull bolt-action for increased rate-of-fire compared to its contemporaries, giving it the nickname Ruck-Zuck (German slang for \
Karabinek wzór 1935

Pistolet maszynowy wzór 1934
Steyr-Solothurn wz. 34
While a German design by Louis Stange, the  MP 34 was mainly produced by Steyr and was adopted by the Austro-Hungarian Army. As common with early submachine guns, this design was heavy, expensive, but reliable, with a horizontal magazine using the potent 9x25mm Mauser cartridge.
Pistolet maszynowy wzór 38
Zbrojovka Brno wz. 38
Seeing the need for a submachine-gun using the standard pistol cartridge of the Army, the Monarchy would adopt the ZK 383, designed by brothers Josef and František Koucký at Zbrojovka Brno, as the MP 38. Using a horizontal magazine like the MP 34, the ZK 383 is known for having a light bipod, allowing it to be used as a substitute squad automatic weapon. Later models were heavily simplified for cheaper and faster production, along with a vertical foldable magazine like the Danuvia SMGs.
Pistolet maszynowy wzór 1943
Danuvia wz. 43
While the Danuvia 39M proved successful, its folding magazine was not enough to compensate for its bulky design for vehicle crewmen. The 43M redesign with a folding metal stock and a new, more reliable foldable magazine design allowed it to be much more compact during transport or storage, without sacrificing firepower.

Pojazd gasienicowy wzór 1937
PG wz. 37
To observers and theorists, the civil war in America showcased modern mechanised warfare and showed how much tracked vehicles had improved in reliability and cross-country capabilities. As a stop-gap measure to improve the mobility of its motorised infantry, the Armee-Oberkommando assigned numerous artillery tractors, mostly of the Praga T-series, to its rapid divisions, redesignating them as tracked troop transports until proper armoured carriers could be introduced.
Pojazd gasienicowy wzór 1938
PG wz. 38
The Saurer RR-7 was a hybrid armoured vehicle that could use both tracks or wheels. It was introduced as an artillery tractor in 1937, but proved to be a far more versatile vehicle with its light armour and high mobility. Not only would it fill the role of a reconnaissance vehicle, but also that of a troop transport, making it the Monarchy's first armoured vehicle of that type.
Transporter opancerzony wzór 1943
TO wz. 43
With a large number of Nimród self-propelled anti-air vehicles in inventory and its autocannon struggling to fill its role, a large number of them were rebuilt without a turret to fill the role of an armoured ambulance and troop carrier. These made them one of the first modern armoured personnel carriers and significantly lengthening the service life of its parent design.

Rifle No.1 Mk III
No.1 Mk III
Rifle No.4 Mk I
No.4 Mk I
After the revolution and civil war it became apparent that the No.3 Enfield was not fit for urban combat. Trials for an entirely new rifle were finally held in 1932, but would largely fail to bear fruit, with new designs unable to match the old Enfield or receiving unfavourable troop evaluations. In light of this showing, Enfield replacement efforts were ultimately shelved. In 1935, however, a joint effort between The Revolutionary Small Arms factory in Enfield and Birmingham Small Arms was presented to the government. The new rifle was a development on the old Lee-Enfield with a shorter barrel, side mounted sling, and a simplified bolt system. While similar to its predecessors, the No.4 is stronger and easier to produce, though a bit heavier due to its barrel. An interesting fact about the No.4 is that it comes with an aperture sight that is set at increments of 100 yds (91m); calibrated for 200-1,300 yards (183-1189 metres).

Ross Mk III
Initially adopted in 1903, the Ross rifle saw several upgrades before the Great War, culminating in the Mk III in 1910. Initially a symbol of patriotism among Canadians as the British refused to license the Lee-Enfield to them, the rifle received an abysmal reputation within the first year of the war, unable to handle the lower quality of British-made ammunition and significantly worse with trench conditions than its contemporaries, and was officially pulled out of service in 1916. Nevertheless, the SMLE was never produced in Canada before the British Revolution, prompting its unwanted return to service as a stopgap until production of the SMLE could begin at Long Branch, Ontario.
Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the loyal Dominions only had access to one arsenal capable of producing its service rifle. Some British engineers from Enfield took refuge in Ontario. With the help of the Lithgow factory in Australia, they continued their work on upgrading the venerable SMLE, resulting in the Mk V. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the receiver instead of the middle, improving sighting radius, accuracy, and speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.
Carbine, Machine, Owen Mk I
Owen Gun
With the prospect of war coming, the Army has requested a submachine gun to be developed for close quarters and urban warfare, especially with the destructiveness that characterises the civil war in America. The Canadian Army officially adopted a weapon designed by young Australian private Evelyn Owen. The 
Rifle, Self-Loading, Long Branch Mk I
SLR Mk I
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-Tank, Mk I
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I (PIAT) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.
PIAT
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the Canadian Army during the Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the Canadian Army has recommissioned them back into service.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Vickers M Mk II & ML 3 inch mortar
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG features a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It has been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar is a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it is reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Vickers M Mk III & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar is a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.
Receivers, Tabby, Periscope Mk 1
Tabby Scope
The Tabby Scope is one of the Canadian government's most closesly guarded secrets; the process of development is even hidden from the fellow members of the Entente. A chance discovery by scientists in the ISAC, the scope is shaped in the form of a canteen but contains a monocular infrared scope along with a battery. Assigned to only the most elite of the elite, the scope allows the unprecedented advantage of having vision in the dark.
Carbine, Machine, Thompson Mk I
The British Army became vaguely interested in the American submachine guns in around 1925 when Auto-Ordnance attempted to secure a licensing deal with Birmingham Small Arms. The British Revolution sabotaged the deal, but interest was picked back up by the Canadian Army, which secured the licensing of the M1926 to produce at home, albeit in small numbers until the outbreak of civil war in the United States.
Carbine, Machine, Lanchester Mk I
With the outbreak of all out war in the United States and the looming clouds of war in Europe, the Canadian Army desperately needs arms for the homecoming. With little time to prepare and research, the Canadian Army has commissioned the 'Lanchester' submachine gun, a near perfect copy of the German MP-28.
Carbine, Commando, De Lisle Mk I
What began as a private venture by engineer William Godfray de Lisle soon caught the attention of the RCMP's Special Branch looking to equip agents with improved weaponry. The De Lisle carbine is, essentially, a shortened SMLE converted to .45 ACP, a subsonic cartridge with an integrated suppressor. The suppressor and ammunition together produced as little as 85.5 decibels when firing, making it very suitable for commando operations.

Ruskin Rhino
Rhino

Hippo

The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

A4 'Reliant'

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Sd.Kfz. 11
The Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11 was developed from 1934 to 1938 and involved Borgward AG and, from 1937 onwards, the design became associated with Hannoversche Maschinenbau AG. Initially intended to be a prime mover, the Sd.Kfz. 11 soon was transferred to the role of a transporter, when the need for an all-terrain vehicle became apparent. Capable of carrying eight soldiers in addition to towing a gun or a trailer, the truck became the main vehicle of panzergrenadiers.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251
Sd.Kfz. 251
Developed on the chassis of Sd.Kfz. 11, the Schützenpanzerwagen Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251 half-tracks (commonly referred to as \
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 256
Sd.Kfz. 256
In late 1943, the German Army asked Auto-Union to develop a light, fully tracked reconnaissance vehicle, capable of carrying 6-8 men in an open-top superstructure. Introduced in 1944 the Schwerer Schützenpanzerwagen 'Kätzchen' (Heavy Armoured Personnel Carrier 'Kitten') had the hull's shape similar to the hull of the Stu.Pz.Kpfw. VII, but much smaller. The vehicle has front wheel drive with five or six overlapping steel road wheels and was designed to attain speeds in excess of 50 km/hr and has light armour. Warmly met by the German Military despite not very high speed, the vehicle was adopted in service under the name Sd.Kfz. 256.
Leichter Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 222
Sd.Kfz. 222
Introduced in 1936, the Leichter Panzerspähwagen ('light armoured reconnaissance vehicle') Sd. Kfz. 222 is a modernised version of the Sd. Kfz. 221. Being a road armoured car, manned by a three-man crew and has 4-wheel drive, the Sd.Kfz. 222 armed with a 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon and a 7,92-mm M.G. 13 machine gun. Performing well on the good road network, the reconnaissance vehicle suffered in performance on the rough terrain and was badly received by colonial troops.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, 8-rad, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 231
Sd.Kfz. 231
The Sd.Kfz. 231 Schwerer Panzerspähwagen ('heavy armoured reconnaissance vehicle') is a military designation for a line of reconnaissance armoured cars intended for armoured divisions. Initially developed in 1932 as 6 wheels variants, it was obvious that this variant will be obsolete in the nearest future. In 1937 the 8 wheels variant armed with 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon was introduced in service. The all-wheel drive four-axle Sd.Kfz. 231 are one of the most technically advanced armoured vehicles and are able to operate on rough terrain, with a cross-country comparable to that of tanks.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 234
Sd. Kfz. 234
This new armoured car is seen as a successor to the eight-wheeled Panzerspähwagen Sd.Kfz.231. Among other priorities, better protection and heavier armament were viewed as crucial for the new armoured car. The production vehicle is heavier and bulkier than the Sd.Kfz.231, with mudguards that went along the side from end to end, with four storage compartments located inside. The main version of the vehicle, also known as Sd.Kfz.234/1, is armed with open-top turret fitted with a 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon and coaxial M.G. 34.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 234/2
Sd.Kfz. 234/2
A respectable armoured car, the Sd.Kfz.234/2 (also known as 'Puma') presents great speed and a significant 50 mm KwK.39/1 cannon. With improved frontal armour and formidable main gun, the car can traverse into volatile areas that are generally restricted to other like-minded vehicles. Being the greatly improved variant of the Sd.Kfz.234, the 'Puma' is intended to be used in a 6-strong platoon as a support vehicle. More often, though, commanders use the Sd.Kfz. 234/2 as fast tank hunters.

K.M.St.E. S 53
Malaiische S 53
Ordered in 1921 as a more powerful version of the FMSR Class P to eliminate double-heading of express services, the FMSR Class L was an improved and more powerful 4-6-2 (Pacific) steam locomotive variant intended to haul longer coaches with lesser need for additional motive power. Following the German reorganisation of the FMSR, these locomotives were redesignated as 'S 53'.
K.M.St.E. S 56
Malaiische S 56
Improving upon the highly regarded RSR 260 class operated by Siam, these locomotives made by Hanomag were the ultimate development of the Malayan 4-6-2 (Pacific). Great care was taken to reduce weight wherever possible making it cheap to produce, and were easily able to maintain 80km/hr (50 mph) on the roller coaster profile between Singapur and Gemas. Tests have proven these locomotives capable of 112km/hr (70 mph).
K.M.St.E. S 56 Pz.
Malaiische S 56 P
As railways started to be attacked by enemy aircraft, new locomotives to serve on the fronts need to be able to take hits from up to 20mm cannons. To protect the locomotive for service against air attack, some Malaiische S 56 locomotives are fitted with three-inch-thick armour plates along the locomotive to protect the vital parts.

1891 tslis Mosinis Sistemis Shashkhana
Sh-91
The 1891 Mosin-Nagant, chambered in 7.62x54R, has remained as the standard infantry rifle among Germany’s eastern allies following the collapse of the Russian Empire. With many of them facing chronic shortages of weaponry and unable to source new arms from Russia, local production of the rifle continued in Poland, Ukraine, and other Oststaaten with established arms industries. While somewhat outdated compared to newer Mauser rifles, the Mosin-Nagant remains a cheap and reliable weapon.
1891/27 tslis Mosinis Sistemis Shaskhana
Sh-91/27
The M/27 is a near-total reworking of the original Mosin-Nagant M1891, incorporating extensive improvements to enhance accuracy and durability. While retaining the receiver and magazine of its predecessor, the rifle features upgraded sights, bolt, a new bayonet, and a shorter yet heavy-profile 685mm barrel. Initially developed in Finland, the M/27 has since begun supplanting the older Mosin-Nagant across Eastern Europe, offering their troops a more modern infantry rifle without abandoning existing stockpiles or production lines.
1931 tslis Pistolet-Tqviamprkvevi
PT-31
The Suomi KP/31 is a Finnish submachine gun chambered in the widely used 9x19mm Parabellum cartridge. Entering service in 1931, its innovative design and high rate of fire quickly gained attention across Europe, leading to widespread licensing and local production. In Ukraine, domestic manufacturing has allowed for large-scale exports to Georgia, where the Suomi has gradually replaced the Schmeisser MP-28 as the standard submachine gun. With a fire rate of up to 900 rounds per minute and a high-capacity drum magazine, it provides exceptional firepower in close combat. However, its considerable weight and demanding maintenance remain notable drawbacks.
1940 tslis Tvitmteni Shashkhana
Sh-40
At the onset of the Second Weltkrieg, the Russian army deployed vast numbers of the SVT-40 semi-automatic rifle, giving their forces a significant edge in firepower. Isolated from the rest of Europe, Georgia found itself without a self-loading rifle of its own, making captured SVTs a highly desirable asset. After successfully reverse-engineering the design, the Georgian arms industry was able to produce a small number of these rifles for the Armed Forces.
1928 tslis Shmaiseris Sistemis Pistolet-Tqviamprkvevi
PT-28
While the submachine gun concept was well-proven during the latter phase of the Weltkrieg, many nations see serious shortage of the weapon type. Unable to secure the German Army's standard MP-40, the Oststaaten often have to make due with what is commercially available through private firms. The MP-28, an improvement of the earlier MP-18 designed by Hugo Schmeisser, is a popular choice thanks to its relative ruggedness and affordability, along with Schmeisser's willingness to licence the design.
1939 tslis Mosinis Sistemis Karabini
K-39
As the Weltkrieg drew near, there arose an urgent need for a cheap and reliable weapon for rear echelon troops. For the Oststaaten, the solution came in the form of a vastly shortened version of the Mosin-Nagant rifle. Drawing inspiration from the Model 1907 carbine and incorporating the design advancements of the Finnish M/27, the K-39 was developed. Featuring a shortened barrel and stock, the K-39 carbine measures just 1000 mm in total length, making it more compact and easier to handle for non-frontline personnel.
