Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
SMLE Mk III
The venerable Lee-Enfield (SMLE) rifle that served the Empire throughout the Great War continues to be the standard arm of the Dominions following the Empire's collapse. Quantities of the SMLE rifle can be found in arsenal throughout the many Dominions. With rifle production seriously disrupted with the loss of the arms factories of England, the Dominions have turned to the old reliable three-oh-three as their standard weapon of choice until suitable replacements are created or purchased.
SMLE Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the need to rearm what remained of the Empire became paramount. British engineers from the RSAF (Royal Small Arms Factory) began taking refuge in Ontario to work on an existing Lee-Enfield. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the side instead of the middle, which features improved sighting radius, sighting accuracy, and sighting speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.
Owen Mk 1
Owen Machine Carbine
With the prospect of war coming, the Army has requested a submachine gun to be developed for close quarters and urban warfare, especially with the destructiveness that characterises the civil war in America. The Canadian Army officially adopted a weapon designed by young Australian private Evelyn Owen. The 
Long Branch Mk 1
SLR Mk 1
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat. Many copies were sent to arm the Dominions of the Empire in preparation for the eventual homecoming.
Receivers, Tabby, Periscope Mk 1
Tabby Scope
The Tabby Scope is one of the Canadian government's most closesly guarded secrets; the process of development is even hidden from the fellow members of the Entente. A chance discovery by scientists in the ISAC, the scope is shaped in the form of a canteen but contains a monocular infrared scope along with a battery. Assigned to only the most elite of the elite, the scope allows the unprecedented advantage of having vision in the dark.
BSA Thompson M1926
The Canadian Army has formally adopted the iconic American submachine gun associated with crime and vice. The M1928A1 is the service variant of the classic M1921 Thompson Machine gun, and although production is expensive, the gun is well-liked among the troops in the field who praise its efficiency and reliability. While most variants rely on smaller 'box' magazines, some other variants feature large and bulky fifty-round drum magazines.
Lanchester Submachine Gun
With the outbreak of all out war in the United States and the looming clouds of war in Europe, the Canadian Army desperately needs arms for the homecoming. With little time to prepare and research, the Canadian Army has commissioned the 'Lanchester' submachine gun, a near perfect copy of the German MP-28.
Canadian Machine Carbine Mk 1
In further cooperation with the Small Arms Ltd at Long Branch, the Canadian military has officially adopted a new compliment and perhaps successor to the Owen. The Machine Carbine Mk 1 features a selective fire trigger with an innovative horizontal magazine to withstand dirt, dust, and sand. Pressure on the top of the trigger would fire the weapon in fully automatic, while pressure on the bottom would fire semi-automatic. While the new machine carbine features a lower rate of fire than the Owen, its reliability and ability to deal selective fire make it quite the potential successor. Many quantities found their way into the arsenal of the Dominions.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-tank No. 68
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I (PIAT) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.
PIAT
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the Canadian Army during the Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the Canadian Army has recommissioned them back into service.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Vickers M Mk II & ML 3 inch mortar
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG features a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It has been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar is a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it is reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Vickers M Mk III & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar is a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.

Ruskin Rhino
Rhino
With the success of the Rover under its belt, the Ruskin Motor Bodies company designed the Rhino in response to an Australasian government request for a substantially heavier armoured car built upon the CMP chassis. Though substantial changes were made to that CMP chassis, after much work the Rhino was born, and with substantial interchangeability of parts with other CMP-based designs to boot.
Ruskin Hippo
Hippo
To accommodate a substantially larger gun, the Rhino's coaxial machine had to be removed and its turret slightly enlarged. The 'Hippo' as this model has been designated, proved to be quite the headache for its design team, as these changes caused the already heavy vehicle to run into weight issues like its predecessors which had to be slowly worked out prior to the vehicle's adoption.

Arisaka Type 38
Type 38
Adopted by the Imperial Japanese Army in 1905, the '38th year 'of the Meiji Period (hence its name); the Type 38 uses the 6.5x50mm Arisaka cartridges. This cartridge produces little recoil when fired. The Type 38 at 128 cm is a long rifle due to the emphasis on bayonet training for Japanese soldiers of this era.
Arisaka Type 99
Type 99
The Type 99 is intended to serve as a replacement for the Type 38. The Imperial Army recognised the weaknesses of the Type 38 and pushed for the Type 99 to serve as its replacement. The Type 99 was produced in four versions: the regular issue rifle, the 'long' rifle, a paratroop rifle, and a sniper variant.
Kawamura Type 14
Type 14
In 1931, the Japanese Army approached Kijiro Nambu to design a new semi-automatic rifle. His design, based on the Pedersen Rifle, which had been unsuccessfully presented to Japan years earlier, ultimately led nowhere due both to difficulties with the Pedersen delayed-blowback toggle-lock system and the unrealistic goals of the Army. However, two refined rifles based upon the Pedersen system would resurface several years later. The design of Masaya Kawamura, of the Nippon Special Steel Company, would ultimately be chosen and adopted by Japan as the Type 14 due to its reliability and ability to function without the need for wax-lubricated cartridges.
Yokosuka Type 4
Type 4
Japan has long relied on bolt action rifles, and their age is beginning to show. Thus, with the Type 14, the Imperial Army at Yokosuka Naval Arsenal has designed the Type 4 semi-automatic rifle. Charged with two 5-round stripper clips, Type 4 comes in two variants: the navy and the other for the army. The development of this weapon would give the Japanese infantryman significantly more firepower on the battlefield than previous weapons.
Type 97 Anti-Tank Rifle
Given the fact that German tanks exist in China as well as the ever-present threat of the Russian bear on the borders, Type 97 was designed to combat armoured vehicles. The gun is a monstrosity of a weapon; it has an overall length of 2.09 metres and weighs 52 kilograms (115 pounds). Capable of penetrating 30mm of armour at a range of 250 metres; it is truly a fearsome weapon in the right hands.
Type 4 70mm anti-tank Rocket Launcher
The rocket launcher was made in two parts that are to be joined in the middle. Intended to be fired while lying down prone; the Type 4 rocket launcher uses a bipod and a mortar fuze. Interestingly enough, the Type 4 rocket uses angled venturis in the base to spin the rocket for stability.
Type 3 Heavy Machine Gun & Type 11 mortar
The Type 3 Heavy Machine Gun was based on the design of the French Hotchkiss M1914. The Type 3 uses an ammo strip for loading rounds, and its tripod could be used for anti-aircraft mounting. The Type 11 mortar was developed in the 11th year of the Taisho Emperor. Exceptional amongst Japanese weapons; Type 11 is a muzzle-loaded weapon with a rifled bore.
Type 11 LMG & Type 10 grenade discharger
Combat experience in the Russo-Japanese War of 1905 taught the Imperial Army the utility of machine guns providing covering fire. The Type 11 was developed by the famed arms designer Kijiro Nambu based on a French Hotchkiss M1909 and is air-cooled and gas-operated. A unique feature about the Type 11 machine gun is its detachable hopper; it can be refilled while attached and does not require removal during operation. The Type 10 grenade discharger was a smoothbore muzzle-loaded weapon with an incredible range of 175 metres.
Type 99 LMG & Type 94 mortar
The Type 99 LMG uses the new rounds found in the Arisaka Type 99. Retaining the same design as previous light machine guns and bears a resemblance to the English Enfield-Automatic. The Type 99 also allows for the attachment of a standard-issue bayonet and 2.5x telescopic sight for marksmanship. The Type 94 mortar is a heavy mortar that has a range of 4000 yards. It is intended to serve as an artillery piece that could be hauled in pieces to remote areas.
Type 1 HMG & Type 2 12 cm Mortar
The Type 1 is a meatgrinder of a weapon. This gas operated heavy machine gun can also be fielded as an anti-aircraft weapon. The Type 2 mortar is virtually identical to the Stokes-Brandt 81mm mortar with the only difference being that the bipod legs of the Type 2 can be removed.
Model 2 submachine gun
Designed by famed arms designer Kijiro Nambu, the Model 2 is a blowback-operated submachine gun. One of Japan's first attempts at a submachine gun, the Model 2 features an incredible high rate of fire while also being light and portable. A pnuematic buffer device cushions the action of the gun and acts as a bolt delay.
Type 100
Type 100 is Japan's first mass-produced submachine gun. Although there are variants with bipods and increased sights for cavalry units, it is designed for infantry use. The gun fires 8mm at 450 rounds per minute. Interestingly some variants have been converted to folding stocks for use by army paratroopers.
Type 100/44
This 1944 modification of Type 100 includes several changes designed to simplify the weapon. The 1944 variant is longer than the earlier Type 100 with simple iron sights and a greatly simplified muzzle. The firing rate of the 1944 version is also considerably higher, with the gun firing at 800 rounds per minute. The gun is an absolute monster at close ranges with satisfactory accuracy and low recoil.

Japanese Government Railways Class D51
JGR Class D51
While the Class D50 was well liked, the financial situation of the early 1930s led to no new freight locomotive designs until 1936. The latest freight locomotive design, the Class D51 had numerous improvements, from the use of welding, implementing a higher pressure boiler, revised cylinders, reduced axle load and overall shortening the locomotive, it proved a great step up from its predecessor.
Japanese Government Railways Class D52
JGR Class D52
Based on the D51 design, the D52 incorporated numerous austerity modifications to improve the performance of the locomotive and reduce the cost of production. The design has a larger boiler and grate area, leading to higher tractive effort, while austerity considerations allow for replacing the running board with wood and looser production standards for most components.
Imperial Japanese Army Type 94 Armoured Train
IJA Type 94
Based on lessons learned from the Improvised Armoured Train, the Type 94 can achieve up to 60 kmph and has a range of 150 km. Mainly designed to haul artillery wagons and specialised armoured trains, the train was not well liked by both engine crews or servicemen.
Imperial Japanese Army Type 90 365mm Railway Gun
IJA Type 90
A worn gun purchased from the IJN, the reliable Vickers 14 inch/45 naval gun was refurbished and used briefly as coastal artillery before being placed on a special artillery carriage. The railway gun was designed with 360 degree traverses and had an auxiliary engine, giving limited autonomy to the gun.

Bunduqiat Almsha Namudhaj 1898
Mauser B98
Mauser G98 was the main service rifle of the Ottoman Army during the Weltkrieg. The Ottoman M1903, chambered in 7.65mm, was the most common variant of Mauser in use. Pronounced 'Mavzer' by the Ottomans, this iconic rifle achieved great fame and became deeply ingrained in the Turkish subconscious.\n'One side of me withered from sorrows / the other from a Mavzer wound'. Freed from Ottoman control, the Palestinian army possess a large number of these rifles.

Mauser G98

Pronounced 'Mavzer' by the Ottomans, this iconic rifle achieved great fame and became deeply ingrained in the Turkish subconscious.\n'One side of me withered from sorrows / the other from a Mavzer wound'. Le fusil Mle 1902 Indochinois
Mle 1902 Indochinois
Lighter and easier to handle and load than the Mle 1886/M93 Lebel rifle, the Berthier fusil Mle 1902 'Indochinois' rifles proved more suitable for offhand shooting and easier to maintain in tropical environments, and saw its use in Indochina.

Rifle No.1 Mk III*
No.1 Mk III*
Alongside the German Mauser, the Lee-Enfield is perhaps one of the most recognisable rifles in service with any military. Although the No.1 Mk III* saw use in the Legation Cities well before the Weltkrieg in the hands of the old British garrison, the revolution in 1926 and the retreat of the China Station to Australasia in the same year saw the rifle continue service with police forces across the Legation Cities, as well as with the Shanghai Volunteer Corps.\n\nAs most of these rifles were Weltkrieg-era surplus, and many have long been without proper maintenance or replaceable parts, attrition has taken its toll. Now that there may be too few rifles to furnish an expanded police force, some have come to regard the Lee-Enfield as necessarily obsolete.
Mauser 98b
Mauser 98b
The Mauser 98b is the cutting edge of German bolt-action rifle technology. With an easier to operate bolt, superior ironsights, an improved stock, and a lighter build, this weapon is a substantial improvement over its predecessors. The 98b is also especially practical in China, as the most common rifle beyond the Neutral Zone is the Hanyang 88, a copy of the older Mauser 1888, with which it shares ammunition.
Selbstladegewehr 40
Selbstlader 40
Designed by the Mauser company in response to a German Army desire for a self-loading rifle with a backup bolt action, the Selbstladegewehr 40 was a massive leap forward in German small arms design. While initial prototypes were highly inaccurate, heavy, and prone to jamming, the German army eventually dropped some its other specific requirements for the rifle, and the end result, the predecessor the rifle that would ultimately be adopted, proved effective.
Maschinenkarabiner 42
MKb 42
The MKb 42 is a revolutionary design chambered for a new intermediate cartridge, developed by famous German arms designer Hugo Schmeisser in conjunction with the Haenel company. While the first prototype of the rifle was prone to jamming, both the military and even the Kaiser himself were extremely enthusiastic about the project and immense resources were concentrated on improving the design, with the rifle deemed ready for adoption in 1942.

Shanghai 'Feixing Baolei'
'Feixing Baolei'
The 'Feixing Baolei' is a standard armoured riot control vehicle used by the Shanghai police force, and the Shanghai Volunteer Corps is also equipped with a small number of such armoured vehicles.\n\nThis vehicle's carriage can hold more than ten individuals. The top of the carriage is equipped with a machine gun or infantry support gun, with several shooting holes at the rear. The compartment of the armoured vehicle has skylights, allowing the crews to reach out of the compartment, in order to suppress a crowd with strong light or high-pressure water guns. The huge carriage of the armoured vehicle made it look like a prison vehicle, so people gave this unnamed riot control vehicle a derogatory nickname: 'Feixing Baolei' or 'Flying Fortress'. As time passed, even actual prison vehicles in Shanghai received the same name.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 231 8-rad
Sd.Kfz. 231
After the end of the Weltkrieg and collapse of British Empire, the Shanghai Volunteer Corps also began to gradually adopt German-made weapons. The German-owned company The New Engineering & Shipbuilding Co., which was originally responsible for manufacturing armoured vehicles for Shanghai Volunteer Corps, has now started to officially produce German armoured vehicles.\n\nAccording to the opinion of the German military advisors in China, the armoured vehicle uses a eight-wheeled chassis based on the characteristics of the Chinese battlefield.

Springfield Krag-Jørgensen Model 1898
Springfield M1898
The Krag-Jørgensen rifle was adopted by the United States in 1892 to replace the ageing Springfield Model 1884. While the Krags proved superior at close ranges to comparable Mauser designs in the Spanish-American war, the rifle's flimsy action in comparison to newer bolt designs necessitated a replacement a mere decade after its adoption. Liberia acquired its Krags as highly discounted military assistance from the United States shortly after the fall of France in Europe, thanks largely to American fears of Nationalist French invasion of the country.
Fabrique Nationale Mauser Modelo 1924
FN-24
Mosin-Mauser 1891/98/25 m. modelio šautuvas
25 MM
Germany's eastern allies all had large stocks of Russian Mosin rifles, but they all adopted the 8mm Mauser cartridge to match German standards. Wanting to make cost-effective use of existing stocks until more Mauser rifles were available, Polish arsenals worked to convert Mosin rifles into carbines chambering the new official cartridge. The wz. 91/98/25 pattern would quickly be adopted by other Oststaaten to modernise their existing stocks.
Lietuviškas Mauser 1924 m. modelio šautuvas
24 L
The Weltkrieg showed first-hand that full-length rifles were unwieldy in trench warfare with little benefit. Germany's famed assault troops were given the shorter Kar. 98AZ whenever possible, and soon after the war, it was decided to standardise on a carbine-length rifle to replace the G.98. The Kar. 98b, based on the AZ model, differed little from its direct predecessor, but more significantly from the longer G. 98, with improved sights and a turned-down bolt. More importantly, it used more parts from the long rifle to increase parts interchangeability and lower manufacturing costs. Many models were ordered by Lithuanian government.
Bergmann 1935 m. modelio pistoletas-kulkosvaidis
35 B
While the sub-machine gun concept was well-proven during the latter phase of the Weltkrieg, many nations see serious shortage of the weapon type as the Second Weltkrieg broke out. Unable to secure the German Army's standard MP-40, the Oststaaten often have to make due with what is commercially available through private firms. The MP-35, designed by Emil Bergmann and notable for its horizontal magazine on the right side, is a popular choice thanks to its relative ruggedness and affordability, along with Bergmann's willingness to license the design.

Brauer 1943 m. modelio prieštankinis šautuvas

1943 m. modelio prieštankinis raketsvaidis 'Panzerschreck'
43 P
The Raketenpanzerbuchse 54 shoulder-launched anti-tank rocket launchers, nicknamed 'Ofenrohr' ('stove pipe') and the more popular 'Panzerschreck' ('Tank's Fright'), are lightweight infantry weapons developed for the German military, inspired by the rumours about American rocket launchers. Under ideal conditions, the R.Pz.B. 54 can penetrate heavy tanks' armour with its 8,8-centimetre rockets, already used by the Raketenwerfer 43 rocket launchers. As a drawback, the R.Pz.B. 54 produces a lot of smoke upon firing. Many shipped over from Germany given Lithuania's lack of anti tank weapons.

Kırıkkale nam.55
Kırıkkale T55 was the name given to the Ottoman modification of Mauser Gewehr 98. Starting in 1933, the Ottoman Empire updated their old rifles to a common configuration known as the Model of 1936 and all in 8x57 Mauser cartridge. The standardisation of all Mausers in Ottoman inventory helped ease the logistical nightmare of having to supply 4 different types of rifle ammunition to all units.

Opel Blitz
Opel Blitz mit Wurfrahmen 40

Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Sd.Kfz. 11
The Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11 was developed from 1934 to 1938 and involved Borgward AG and, from 1937 onwards, the design became associated with Hannoversche Maschinenbau AG. Initially intended to be a prime mover, the Sd.Kfz. 11 soon was transferred to the role of a transporter, when the need for an all-terrain vehicle became apparent. Capable of carrying eight soldiers in addition to towing a gun or a trailer, the truck became the main vehicle of panzergrenadiers.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251
Sd.Kfz. 251
Developed on the chassis of Sd.Kfz. 11, the Schützenpanzerwagen Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251 half-tracks (commonly referred to as \
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 256
Sd.Kfz. 256
In late 1943, the German Army asked Auto-Union to develop a light, fully tracked reconnaissance vehicle, capable of carrying 6-8 men in an open-top superstructure. Introduced in 1944 the Schwerer Schützenpanzerwagen 'Kätzchen' (Heavy Armoured Personnel Carrier 'Kitten') had the hull's shape similar to the hull of the Stu.Pz.Kpfw. VII, but much smaller. The vehicle has front wheel drive with five or six overlapping steel road wheels and was designed to attain speeds in excess of 50 km/hr and has light armour. Warmly met by the German Military despite not very high speed, the vehicle was adopted in service under the name Sd.Kfz. 256.

LWS
Land-Wasser-Schlepper II

Leichter Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 222
Sd.Kfz. 222
Introduced in 1936, the Leichter Panzerspähwagen ('light armoured reconnaissance vehicle') Sd. Kfz. 222 is a modernised version of the Sd. Kfz. 221. Being a road armoured car, manned by a three-man crew and has 4-wheel drive, the Sd.Kfz. 222 is armed with a 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon and a 7,92-mm M.G. 13 machine gun. Performing well on the good road network, the reconnaissance vehicle suffered in performance on the rough terrain and was badly received by colonial troops.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, 8-rad, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 231
Sd.Kfz. 231
The Sd.Kfz. 231 Schwerer Panzerspähwagen ('heavy armoured reconnaissance vehicle') is a military designation for a line of reconnaissance armoured cars intended for armoured divisions. Initially developed in 1932 as 6 wheels variants, it was obvious that this variant will be obsolete in the nearest future. In 1937 the 8 wheels variant armed with 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon was introduced in service. The all-wheel drive four-axle Sd.Kfz. 231 are one of the most technically advanced armoured vehicles and are able to operate on rough terrain, with a cross-country comparable to that of tanks.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 234
Sd. Kfz. 234
This new armoured car is seen as a successor to the eight-wheeled Panzerspähwagen Sd.Kfz.231. Among other priorities, better protection and heavier armament were viewed as crucial for the new armoured car. The production vehicle is heavier and bulkier than the Sd.Kfz.231, with mudguards that went along the side from end to end, with four storage compartments located inside. The main version of the vehicle, also known as Sd.Kfz.234/1, is armed with open-top turret fitted with a 2-cm Kw.K. 30 L/55 autocannon and coaxial M.G. 34.
Schwerer Panzerspähwagen, Sonderkraftfahrzeug 234/2
Sd.Kfz. 234/2
A respectable armoured car, the Sd.Kfz.234/2 (also known as 'Puma') presents great speed and a significant 50 mm KwK.39/1 cannon. With improved frontal armour and formidable main gun, the car can traverse into volatile areas that are generally restricted to other like-minded vehicles. Being the greatly improved variant of the Sd.Kfz.234, the 'Puma' is intended to be used in a 6-strong platoon as a support vehicle. More often, though, commanders use the Sd.Kfz. 234/2 as fast tank hunters.
10,5-cm leichte Feldhaubitze Modell 33
10,5-cm le.F.H. 33
The 10,5-cm le.F.H. 33 is the standard artillery piece of the Deutsches Heer, adopted for service in 1933 and used by all divisions and artillery battalions. This gun was developed in 1928-29 by Rheinmetall, and was a major advance on its predecessor the 10,5-cm le.F.H. 16 - a completely new three-point split trail gun carriage provides more stability and increases the traverse to 56 degrees alongside with the sighting mechanism to make it easier to fire at moving targets.
15-cm schwere Feldhaubitze Modell 36
15-cm s.F.H. 36
The 15-cm schwere Feldhaubitze 36, nicknamed Immergrün ('Evergreen'), is the basic division-level heavy howitzer, serving alongside the smaller but more numerous 10,5-cm le.F.H. 33. The gun originated with a contest between Rheinmetall and Krupp, both of whom entered several designs that were all considered unsatisfactory for one reason or another. In the end the army decided the solution was to combine the best features of both designs, using the Rheinmetall gun on a Krupp carriage. In 1935 the Army drafted a requirement for a lightened version of the gun which could be towed by a single horse team in one piece, for which a new proposed design from Rheinmetall was selected.
17-cm Kanone Modell 40 in Mörserlafette
17-cm K. 40 Mrs.Laf.
The 17-cm Kanone 40, built by Krupp and Hannoversche Maschinenbau, is heavy corps-level gun. In the field, the gun delivers great damage, but also has a great disadvantage of requiring a long period of time to tear down for transport. A notable innovation on the 17-cm K. 40 is the \
3,7-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 36
3,7-cm Pa.K. 36
Design of a horse-drawn, 3,7-cm anti-tank gun (designated 3,7-cm Pa.K. 28) by Rheinmetall commenced in 1924. By the early 1930s, it became apparent that horse-drawn artillery is obsolete, and the gun was modified for motorised transport, allowing it to be towed at highway speeds. Nicknamed by soldiers 'Heeresanklopfgerät' ('army door-knocking device'), the re-designated 3,7-cm Pa.K. 36 is the main anti-tank weapon of Deutsches Heer and easily can manage with enemy light tanks.
7,5-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 40
7,5-cm Pa.K. 40
The 7,5-cm Pa.K. 40 was developed after reports of new enemy tank designs began to reach Berlin in 1939. The project was initially given low priority, but following the growing tensions with France and the appearance of heavily armoured tanks, it was given an increased priority. In terms of armour-piercing action, the Pa.K. 40 is superior to the similar guns, but have less effective rollback damping systems, as a result of which the gun is behind analogues to quickly change position.
12,8-cm Panzerabwehrkanone Modell 43
12,8-cm Pa.K. 43
The 12,8-cm Pa.K. 43 is the new anti-tank gun developed initially as a field gun known as the Kanone 43. However, once heavier tanks started to appear the design requirements were altered to include an anti-armour role. The 12,8-cm Pa.K. 44 has a good anti-tank performance over long to extreme-long ranges - 1,800-2,700 metres and beyond - while also doubling as an effective field gun when firing high-explosive shells. But since it was not possible to install a 12,8-cm barrel on the carriage of a conventional anti-tank gun, a special carriage for the gun was designed. The high profile of the gun had to be maintained, which makes the gun extremely visible.
2-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 36
2-cm Fla.K. 36
The 2-cm Fla.K. 30 automatic anti-aircraft cannon was developed by Rheinmetall in 1930, and the Deutsches Heer began to receive guns in 1933. In 1936, due to the growth of tensions, Mauser modernised the gun - the upgraded model received the designation 2-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone 36 and was adopted by the German army. The principle of operation of the Fla.K. 36 mechanisms remains the same - the use of recoil force with a short barrel stroke, but otherwise is similar with only increased the rate of fire to 220 rounds per minute and slightly lowered overall weight to 420 kg.
3,7-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 37
3,7-cm Fla.K. 37
The original 3,7-cm gun was developed by Rheinmetall in 1935. The cannon had an overall length of 89 calibres, which allowed a 4,800 metres maximum ceiling. The Fla.K. 35 was only produced in small numbers, and production had already ended in 1936. Development continued, focusing on replacement of the existing cumbersome dual-axle mount with a lighter single-axle one, resulting in a 3,7-cm Fla.K. 36 with the practical rate of fire raised to 120 rounds per minute. A new, simplified sighting system introduced the next year led to the otherwise-identical 3,7-cm Fla.K. 37 which quickly became the main variant of the gun for the German Army.
8,8-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone Modell 41
8,8-cm Fla.K. 41
Initially developed in 1930 by Krupp the 8,8-cm Flugzeugabwehrkanone 30, commonly known as 'acht-acht' ('eight-eight'), was later modified by Rheinmetall. The versatile carriage allows the 8,8-cm Fla.K. to fire in a limited anti-tank mode when still on its wheels, and many consider the 8,8-cm Fla.K. line to be one of the best of its time. The Rheinmetall Fla.K. 41 is a second generation 8,8-cm gun, which some generals say is 'almost equal to the 12,8-cm cannon'. While having good characteristics, the Fla.K. 41 has problems in service, and is used mainly where it can be properly maintained.
28/32-cm Schweres Wurfgerät Modell 40
s.W.G. 40
Initially designed for Nebeltruppen (Smoke Troops) for smokescreen setting, the schwere Wurfgerät 40 exists in two variants - with wooden and steel frames. The open frames of the most common steel version have short hinged legs to adjust elevation and are sized to fit the 32-centimetre rocket, but adapter rails are provided to allow the 28-centimetre rockets to fit. The s.W.G. 40 also can be mounted on schwere Wurfrahmen 40 frames attached to the sides of different vehicles.
21-cm Raketenwerfer Modell 42
R.W. 42
The 21-cm Raketenwerfer 42 is a five-barreled multiple rocket launcher mounted on the towed carriage derived from that of the 3,7 cm Pa.K. 36 anti-tank gun. The launchers are organised into batteries of six with three batteries per battalion. Intended to provide the fire support and settle smokescreens, the R.W. 42 are mainly used as rocket artillery and almost don't participate in chemical warfare.

Mauser Modelo 1902
Mauser M02
The Mauser Model 1902 is one of several Mauser rifles in service with the Mexican military, but is currently by far the most common. Chambered in 7x57mm, the Model 1902 is a highly modified Spanish Model 1893 Mauser integrating several parts and design elements from the later Mauser Model 1898. The Model 1902, which was purchased in large quantities from the German company DWM, is nearly identical to the domestically produced Model 1910.
Mondragón Modelo 1929
Mondragón M29
The revolutionary Mondragón Model 1908 was among the first semi-automatic rifles to see combat, even serving with the German Luftstreitkräfte during the Weltkrieg. While effective, the Mondragón has always suffered reliability issues and is expensive to produce. The Model 1929 is a modernised and easier to manufacture version of the original Mondragón.

Mendoza Fusil de Asaulto Model 1943
Mendoza FA-M43
The Fusil de Asaulto Model 1943 is the latest design from Socialist gunsmith Rafael Mendoza. It is a magazine fed automatic rifle chambered in a unique shortened derivative of the standard Mexican 7×57mm Mauser cartridge.

Hanyang Type 88
Hanyang Type 88
Originally a product of late Guangxu era 'Self Strengthening' efforts, the Type 88 was designed as a copy of the German Gewehr 88 with export modifications. While collectively inferior to newer rifles despite improvements, and often poorly maintained, the weapon is known to be rugged and reliable if unwieldy.\n\nThis variety is mainly produced at the Hanyang Arsenal, the model's place of design, though equipment shortages often see it used alongside non-standard weapons.
Hanyang Type 27
Hanyang Type 27
Officially called the 'Type 27', this rifle was put into service in 1935 thanks to German assistance after the Qing Restoration. The weapon is functionally a near copy of the German Mauser M1924, and a considerable improvement over the much older Type 88 in terms of weight and recoil.\n\nThis variety is mainly produced at the Hanyang Arsenal with German tools, though it may not fully replace its predecessors for some time.
Tsingtao MP18
Tsingtao MP18
Thanks to the flood of Weltkrieg surplus into the world market in the early 1920s, the MP18 saw action in China some time before a modified version began production in Tsingtao.\n\nWith changes to the magazine and stock, this particular product of Sino-German cooperation has been copied and sold throughout Northern and Southern China, though primarily to the Qing Government and warlords in coastal areas.
Hanyang Type 35
Hanyang Type 35
The Type 35 is a semi-automatic rifle based on the General Liu rifle from 1914. While appearing similar, the Type 35 is shorter, has the rear of the receiver covered, and the gas-trap system replaced by a simple gas port. The new rifle is also no longer able to switch to manual action and uses magazines from the Bohemian ZH-29 rifle and ZB-26 light machine gun.

Nanjing Type Zhongyuan
Zhongyuan
Following the Qing restoration, Benz AG and the Qing government established several automobile factories in China as joint ventures; the most famous being the Zhongyuan Automobile Plants located in Shanghai and Nanjing. Thousands of Type Zhongyuan trucks were produced in these two areas, which entered service in various armies loyal to the Zhili clique.\n\nNotably, the Zhongyuan is not named after the ancient Chinese place name Zhongyuan (the area surrounding the empire's core), but rather the painted logo on the front of the truck's cab; a huge Chinese character 'Zhong' enclosed in a circle.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 11
Haoxu 11
The Sd.Kfz. 11 (Sonderkraftfahrzeug - special motorised vehicle) was a German half-track that saw very widespread use in the Second Weltkrieg. It could carry eight troops in addition to towing a gun or trailer.
Sonderkraftfahrzeug 251
Haoxu 251
The Sd.Kfz. 251 half-track was an armoured fighting vehicle designed and first built by the Hanomag company during the Second Weltkrieg, based on their earlier Sd.Kfz. 11 vehicle.

Espingarda 6,5 mm m/1904
Mauser-Vergueiro
A bolt action rifle with a long history of service, the 6,5 mm m/1904 is more commonly known by the moniker of 'Mauser-Vergueiro', as the main design of the gun is essentially that of the Mauser 98 (with a special bolt action mechanism heavily based on the Gewehr 1888) with the 6.5×58mm Vergueiro cartridge, one developed specifically for the Portuguese variant. It became the official weapon of the Portuguese Army in 1904 and continued in that capacity until the 1940s, being widely used both at home and overseas. While it was not used by Portuguese troops in the western front, as those were armed by Entente, chiefly British, supplies, it saw extensive use in Africa from Portugal, its allies in South Africa, and was also used by Germans who occasionally captured supplies in Portuguese East Africa.
Espingarda 7.92 mm m/1904/36
Mauser-Vergueiro M36
Fundamentally little more than an updated version of the Mauser-Vergueiro, it was introduced to Africa amid rising tensions between Portugal and Germany. A significant amount of these weapons later found themselves under use by the forces of the states in former Portuguese Africa.
Carabina C.A.P Modelo 01
C.A.P M40
A prototype carbine that saw little widespread use in Portugal's army as a whole was the country's first attempt at a standard issue, nationally produced design. As such, the nationalist Integralist junta supported the development of the C.A.P carbine intensely, but by the time it was ready for production, realities on the ground made it more worthwhile to switch to the FBP overall directly from the older Mauser-Vergueiro and variants already widely in use by the armed forces.
Pistola-Metralhadora FBP M/945
FBP M/945
The FBP submachine gun is named after the acronym for the Braço de Prata Factory, the manufacturer for the weapon, a foundry owned and controlled by the Portuguese government itself. While its development started far before its widespread use among foot troops in Portugal's army, the finished model entered production too late to replace the Mauser-Vergueiro in the war. This intent was made clear by the submachine gun having, rather unusually, bayonet sockets compatible with those of the Mauser models used by Portugal throughout the conflicts of the previous decades. The so called 'machine gun pistol' (The term in Portuguese for submachine guns in general) saw extensive use throughout many years, albeit the 1945 variant fell out of use in favour of versions with semi-automatic firing and imported models, as the scale of warfare decreased in later decades after the Second Weltkrieg. While few of the models made it overseas when compared to the old Mauser-Vergueiro, the government of Mozambique was capable of starting production domestically relatively swiftly after decolonisation.
Metralhadora Vickers & Morteiro Stokes
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the British Empire during the Weltkrieg. During its rearmament after the conflict, the restored Portuguese monarchy purchased and fabricated them with the intention of widespread use among infantry both at home and overseas.
Metralhadora Lewis Modelo DEMS & Morteiro SBML de 2 Polegadas
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis served the Entente since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. The MKIII variant had a pistol grip that allowed it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML, while small, is incredibly portable and had greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Metralhadora Vickers Modelo II & Morteiro ML de 3 Polegadas
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG featured a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It had been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar was a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it was reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Metralhadora Vickers-Vedras & Morteiro de 4.2 Polegadas
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar was a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.
Morteiro-Bombarda
Developed in Canada and sold in substantial amounts to Portugal, the mortar design was that of a 'spigot' mortar. Arguing that the design could serve as both an artillery piece and an anti-tank weapon, the design was eagerly adopted by several allies of the Entente, including Portugal. Many ended up stranded in its former overseas possessions.
LATI
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the LATI (A direct translation of its Canadian English name) was developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device was well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there were concerns about its intense recoil.

Rifle, Calibre .30-06, Model 1903
M1903 Springfield
The Model 1903 was developed at Springfield Arsenal when, following the Spanish-American war, the United States deemed the Krag-Jørgensen rifle inadequate on account of its weak action; at its core, the 1903 was an attempt to combine beneficial features of both the Krag and captureds Spanish Mausers. Beginning just before the Weltkrieg, the rifle was gradually adopted along with the .30-06 cartridge, a process that would take several years.
Rifle, Calibre .30-06, Model 1903A1, Special Target
M1903A1 Springfield
The M1903A1 Springfield was adopted by the US Army in 1929 as the 'U.S. Rifle, Calibre .30-06, M1903A1'. It incorporated a pistol-grip, chequered buttplate, serrated trigger, and several smaller improvements into the original 1903 design.
Rifle, Calibre .30 Model 1
M1 Garand
The M1 Garand comes from French-Canadian John Garand at the Springfield Armory. The .30 calibre, gas-operated, 8 shot clip-fed, semi-automatic rifle is designed for simple assemblage and can be field stripped without tools in a few seconds. The semi-automatic operation gives the American rifleman a significant advantage in firepower and shot-to-shot recovery times over enemies armed with bolt action rifles.
Auto-Carbine, Calibre .30, Model 2
M2
The M2 Auto-Carbine is an improvement upon the M1 Garand with a slightly longer receiver than the M1 and modified to accept 20 round Browning Automatic Rifle magazines. The rifle features a fire selector achieved by connector assembly, allowing the weapon to be fired automatically at 700 rpm. In addition to selective fire, the rifle is machined and tapped on the left side with a scope, including the same hardware for rifle grenade mounting.
Anti-Mechanisation Gun, Calibre .50, Model 2, Browning
M2 Anti-Mechanisation Gun
The Second American Civil War necessitated the development of man-portable anti-vehicle weapons. As such, the U.S. Cavalry experimented with lightening and adapting the .50 calibre Browning M2 Heavy Machine Gun for man-portability. While heavier and bulkier compared with its European counterparts, the M2 Anti-Mechanisation Gun was quickly adopted for service after satisfactory performance in Army trials.
Recoilless Rifle, 2.2 inch, Model 18
M18 Recoilless Rifle
The M18 Recoilless Rifle is a shoulder-fired anti-materiel weapon developed by the two engineers 'Kroger' and 'Musser' as part of a U.S. Army Infantry Section freelance project. The M18 was developed in response to rapid improvements in armour which greatly diminished the effectiveness of anti-tank rifles small enough to be man-portable. The M18 Recoilless Rifle has an effective range of 400 yards, a maximum range of nearly 5,000 yards, and is more accurate compared with the old M2. It quickly was nicknamed 'pocket artillery' by the soldiers who used it.
MG, Cal. .30-06, M1919, Browning & Mortar, 3-inch, Stokes
M1919 Browning MG & Stokes mortar
MG, Cal. .50, M2, Browning & Mortar, 2-inch, SMBL
M2 Browning MG & SBML two-inch mortar
MG, Cal. .30-06, M3, Johnson & Mortar, 3.2-inch, M1
M3 Johnson MG & M1 mortar
MG, Cal. .30-06, M5, Johnson & Mortar, 4.2-inch, M2
M5 Johnson MG & M2 mortar
M1 Sniperscope
Developed specifically for the M3 Auto-Carbine, which had special mountings on its receiver for various models of infra-red night-sighting devices, the M1 Sniperscope is the first infrared sniperscope adopted for general use by the U.S. Army. While the M1 has its flaws, like a poor range and an easily damaged Infrared emitter, the M1 still has tremendous value when used correctly.
Infrared Sniperscope, Model 3
M3 Sniperscope
In response to the poor reception of the M1 Sniperscope, the Sniperscope M3 was adopted. With almost twice the effective range of the M1, a more robust infrared light source, and the T23 flash hider, the M3 is a vast improvement over its predecessor, though some soldiers complain of the light source bleeding into the visible spectrum and thus giving away their position.
Carbine, Machine, Thompson Mk I
The British Army became vaguely interested in the American submachine guns in around 1925 when Auto-Ordnance attempted to secure a licensing deal with Birmingham Small Arms. The British Revolution sabotaged the deal, but interest was picked back up by the Canadian Army, which secured the licensing of the M1926 to produce at home, albeit in small numbers until the outbreak of civil war in the United States. As part of New England's ties to the Entente, the classic American submachine gun was among some of the weapons sent to the new Provisional Government.
M50 Reising
Manufactured by Harrington & Richardson Arms Company in Worcester, Massachusetts, Eugene Reising was an assistant to esteemed firearm inventor John M. Browning. As the Civil War raged across America, Reising turned his attention towards developing a submachine gun. A rival to the 1928A1, the M50 is considerably cheaper to produce and features a closed bolt system that gives it more accuracy in semi-automatic fire.
M25
Following the successful adoption of the M1E2 Auto-Carbine, the US Army began searching for a new lightweight rifle to replace the M1 Garand. Developed by Harvey Earle, the M-25 features a reworked gas system along with component parts designed to be fabricated with general tools and machines. The rifle also fires 0.30 ammunition and is incredibly light, a mere 7 pounds. Initial testings have been favourable with many praising the M-25's accuracy over the M1 Garand's.

Fusil Lebel Modèle 1886 modifié 93
Lebel Mle M93
The Mle 1886 Lebel rifle is the first military firearm to use smokeless powder ammunition. This propellant powder, made of stabilised nitrocellulose, is called 
Fusil Berthier Modèle 1915 modifié 32
Berthier Mle M32
After the Weltkrieg, the French military recognised the need to replace the 8mm Lebel cartridge, which is poorly suited to large-capacity rifle magazines and to automatic weapons. After the replacement of the cartridge by the Commune, a modern 7.5mm Mle 1931 rimless cartridge was finally introduced - mostly copying the Communard 7.5mm Mle 1929 cartridge. The Berthier rifle, which quickly became the main rifle of the French army after its retreat to Algeria, was modified in 1932 to use Mauser type magazines and another new cartridge.
Carabine-Mitrailleuses Hotchkiss Type M2
Hotchkiss Mle CMH2
The CMH2 is an ambitiously-engineered and well-built submachine gun submitted by Hotchkiss in 1938 as the Type 017, which entered service as the Carabine-Mitrailleuses Hotchkiss Type M2 (along with its police variant Type M1). The CMH2's extreme compactness is both its best and worst feature - the complexity of having every protruding part fold or retract make the weapon expensive to produce and also reduces ergonomics, which isn't ideal for quick target acquisition.
Fusil MAT Modèle 43
MAT Mle 43
The Fusil MAT Modèle 43 is an automatic rifle, using a 7.5x54mm cartridge and a foldable magazine, which allows it to be a little more compact than its regular counterparts. Along with this, the MAT-43 is produced by machine-stamping process which allows the economical production of large numbers of rifles, then urgently required by the French Government. Despite some issues with rate of fire and reliability, the MAT-43 is considered as the next step in personal weapons.
Mitrailleuse Darne Mle 1919 & Mortier Stokes
Darne Mle 1919 & Mortier Stokes
The Darne Mle 1919 is a light machine gun of French origin. It is gas operated, firing from open bolt in fully automatic only (at an incredible rate of 1100 rounds per minute). The Darne machine gun has an unusual belt feed between the gas piston and barrel, using the two-stage cartridge feed system (cartridge withdrawn from the belt to the rear, and then pushed forward into the barrel). It is chambered in French 7.5mm Mle 1931. The Stokes mortar is a Weltkrieg-era mortar known for its reliability and rate of fire.
Mitrailleuse Hotchkiss Mle 1926 & Mortier Brandt Mle 27/31
The Hotchkiss Mle 1926 served as a replacement for the Darne Mle 1919. While the Hotchkiss's rate of fire was significantly lower than the Darne, its stability and ease of production makes it a more preferred light machine gun. The Brandt mortar is the first in the family of mortars by Edgar Brandt. A simple and effective weapon that has a metal tube to reduce recoil, the Brandt Mortar is able to dish out nearly 18 rounds a minute.
Hotchkiss Mle 1926 & Brandt Mle 27/31
Mitrailleuse Hotchkiss Mle 26/37 & Mortier Brandt Mle 35/38
Hotchkiss Mle 26/37 & Brandt Mle 35/38
The 1937 version of the Hotchkiss machine gun features an increased rate of fire as its predecessor while maintaining the gun's low recoil. The Brandt Mle 35/38 also features improvements to the mortar system to reduce the mortar's recoil and improve its accuracy.
Mitrailleuse Hotchkiss Mle 26/41 & Mortier Brandt Mle 1942
Hotchkiss Mle 26/41 & Brandt Mle 42
The 41 version of the Hotchkiss features a telescopic sight and a slight increase in rate of fire, allowing the gun to be effective in close quarters while maintaining accuracy. The 1942 version of the Brandt mortar has a slight improvement in its rate of fire making it an effective close range support weapon.
BSA M1926 'Thompson Submachine Gun'
As part of the French Exiles' cooperation with the Entente, the French forces have adopted the BSA M1926. The BSA M1926 is the service variant of the classic M1921 Thompson Machine gun, and although production is expensive, the gun is well-liked among the troops in the field who praise its efficiency and reliability. While most variants rely on smaller 'box' magazines, some other variants feature large and bulky fifty-round drum magazines. The gun has been well received and praised by the French infantry who value its incredible stopping power.
Lanchester Submachine Gun
The Lanchester is a submachine gun of Canadian origin, a near carbon copy based on the German MP28. As part of Anglo-French cooperation, number of the Lanchester were sent to National French forces in Africa and loyalist cells on the mainland.
De Lisle Carbine
The De Lisle Carbine has achieved popularity amongst those in the special forces. It is a Frankenstein of a weapon, as it combines features from both the SMLE and the MI1928A1. Fitted with an internal suppressor, the gun is extremely quiet which makes it perfect for partisan action or assassination.
Berliet CBA
Berliet CBA
Laffly S-20 Tracteur, Chassis Long
S-20TL
In 1934, the French army ordered S-35C trucks from Laffly - a cumbersome six-wheel drive vehicle, unpopular with crews. The new Laffly S-20TL, proposed (and adopted) a year later, is generally superior to the previous S-35C, has a better power-to-weight ratio, and offers ample storage boxes in which passengers can store equipment. The truck is recognised as more innovative compared to its Communard analogue, due to only one body type being needed to transport different types of platoons, which allows the S-20TL to be mass-produced in large quantities, even in colonies.
Citroën Tracteur Semi-Chenille P-107
P-107
After the Weltkrieg, Citroën developed a long line of half-tracks based on the Kégresse patent. In 1937, the company introduced its newer and more powerful P-107 model as a successor to the Citroën-Kégresse P-17. The P-107 has two main variants: a light prime mover for artillery guns, and a platform cargo transport for units. Many commanders use them in various standard and non-standard roles, which makes the P-107 a versatile truck.
Berliet Tracteur Chenillé Modèle 39
TC-39
The Tracteur Chenillé Modèle 39, developed by Berliet in 1938 and adopted in 1941 after numerous modifications, is a tracked carrier with a total capacity of ten people: 1 driver and 9 passengers. Unlike the 1938 model, which was equipped with a trailer for transporting main part of troops, the final variant has extended cargo hold with high box-like armoured superstructures with doors at their back, protecting the platoon on the way to the battlefield. Despite rumours that the TC-39 was largely inspired by the designs of Commune (or even a direct copy), it was adopted by the French army.
Véhicule de Transport de Troupes Blindé - Renault
VTTB-R
The Véhicule de Transport de Troupes Blindé - Renault is a closed tracked carrier vehicle developed in 1941, when the Army had doubts that Berliet would be able to meet army specifications for its TC-39. Originally introduced in 1942, the VTTB-R was rejected due to its low capacity and was only accepted into service after increasing the total capacity to 11 people, including the driver. Considered by many specialists to be the one of most modern personnel carriers, the VTTB-R is quite expensive to manufacture.
Schneider-Citroën Auto-Mitrailleuse Amphibie C-40
C-40
The Schneider-Citroën C-40 was first introduced as an amphibious armoured car in 1933, but was rejected by the French government in favour of the AM-80. The project was abandoned until 1939, when the French army requested an amphibious vehicle. Updated as an amphibious transport with a capacity of 10 people, the S-40 was introduced in 1941 and adopted, despite worse characteristics than the Communard counterpart.
Schneider-Citroën Tracteur Chenillé Amphibie Modèle 1943
TCA-43
The TCA-43 is an improved version of the C-40. After initial feedback on the use of the C-40 in the field, the wheels were replaced with tracks, and the cargo bay was expanded along with installation of several mounts for machine guns.
Laffly Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1917/80
AM-80
The Laffly Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1917/80 is a car based on the White-Laffly 1917 heavy armoured car, but updated in 1934 with a new bodywork and engine. Manufactured by Laffly factory in Algeria, the vehicle can climb a 40° slope, and cruise at about 80 km/h on the North African roads, quickly making the AM-80 the main vehicle of the French army, despite the fact that the model is considered outdated.
Citroën Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1940
AM-40
The Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1940, designed by Louis Delagarde to the 1938 specifications of the French army, is a very innovative low profile armoured car with 8-wheel drive and monocoque 2-man body. The process of development of the AM-40 was accompanied by constant design modifications, but with a change in the geopolitical situation, despite the demands of the military to equip the car with a 47-mm cannon, the version with a 20-mm cannon was adopted in 1940.
Citroën Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1942
AM-42
After evaluating the use of the heavy and complex AM-40, the General Staff stated that it needed a lighter and even faster reconnaissance vehicle to patrol colonial areas and perform many other tasks available for cheaper vehicles. Citroën engineers, after a detailed study of Canadian armoured cars, developed the Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1942 to meet the requirements of the army. The new AM-42, a lightweight 4-wheeled vehicle armed with a 20mm cannon, was warmly received by French soldiers.
Citroën Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1942 Canon de 47mm
AM-42-C47
The Auto-Mitrailleuse Modèle 1942 Canon de 47mm is the combined product of Louis Delagarde and other Citroën engineers who installed a 47mm cannon on the AM-40 tower. Like the replacement of the AM-40 in service with the AM-42, the new tower was also mounted on its hull, which made the AM-42 a tank hunter in accordance with the original requirements of the army for the AM-40.
Matériel de 75mm Modèle 1897
Matériel de 75mm
The Matériel de 75mm Mle 1897, also known as the French 75 or 'Soixante-Quinze' ('seventy-five'), is widely regarded as the first modern artillery piece. In service since 1898, the Soixante-Quinze was heavily used in the Weltkrieg, where it was used as an anti-personnel, anti-aircraft and anti-tank weapon. After the retreat to Algeria, the French army continued to use these weapons, mainly because they still mostly meet modern criteria.
Matériel de 105mm Court Modèle 1935 Schneider
Matériel de 105mm C S
The Matériel de 105mm Court Mle 1935 is a conservative design by Schneider. Developed to replace Soixante-Quinze in service, the Matériel de 105mm is more a howitzer than a cannon, and is primarily intended to destroy enemy fortifications.
Canon de 155mm Grande Puissance Filloux Modèle 1917/1942
Canon de 155mm GPF
The Canon de 155mm Grande Puissance Filloux Modèle 1917, also known as the GPF, was developed during the Weltkrieg by Colonel Louis Filloux to meet the urgent need for modern heavy artillery, and is used by both the Communard and the National governments. Due to problems with the production of GPFs in the colonies by the French government, only a limited numbers were in service until 1942, when the GPF design was updated to facilitate production and meet modern standards.
Matériel de 25mm Semi-Automatique Modèle 1934
Matériel de 25mm SA
By the early 1920s, the French Army had come to the realisation that the armour-piercing capability of the 37mm TRP infantry gun would be insufficient against modern tanks. In 1926 Hotchkiss proposed a 25mm gun design, which was not immediately adopted due to financial issues, and the availability of the Matériel de 75mm. The Matériel de 25mm, which entered service only in 1936 with the realisation of the approaching battle for France, is a reliable weapon inspired by the Filloux concept.
Matériel Antichar de 75mm Modèle 1897/1937
Matériel de 75mm AC
With the adoption of the Matériel de 105mm, the old Matériel de 75mm were mainly redesigned into anti-tank guns with an updated chassis. Considered too heavy for modern anti-tank guns, the Soixante-Quinze have higher penetration than their counterparts.
Matériel Antichar de 75mm Semi-Automatique Modèle 1943
Matériel de 75mm AC SA
The Matériel de 75mm SA is a new semi-automatic gun based on the 75mm Matériel de 75mm AC cannon. With a higher rate of fire, the Matériel de 75mm CA SA is still a slightly outdated weapon, but can compete with its modern counterparts, thanks to a modernised mechanism and a new approach to the chassis design.
Mitrailleuse de 25mm Contre-Aéroplanes Modèle 1936
Mitrailleuse 25mm CA
After the retreat to Algeria, the French military announced the need for a specialised anti-aircraft autocannon. Hotchkiss introduced the 25 mm project in 1936, which was adopted due to the slow development of a more promising Schneider variant. Despite the acceptance of the Mitrailleuse 25mm CA into service, the original tripod was replaced to a triangular base with a two-wheel carriage as per the army's request.
Mitrailleuse de 37 mm Contre-Aérien Modèle 1939
Mitrailleuse 37mm CA
Developed since 1930, the Mitrailleuse 37mm CA was finally ready for production in 1939. After a bitter controversy with the French military, who had already adopted the Hotchkiss variant, the Mitrailleuse 37mm CA from Schneider was adopted after initial testing as a more advanced variant than the Hotchkiss's 25mm gun.
Matériel de 75mm Contre-Aérien Modèle 1940
Matériel de 75mm CA
During the late 1920s, it was realised that the Matériel de 75mm Modèle 1897 was outdated as an anti-aircraft weapon and development of a new gun was begun in 1931. The Matériel de 75mm Contre-Aérien Modèle 1940 by Schneider has faster rate of fire, higher muzzle velocity, increased vertical range, modern fire control and greater mobility with new gun carriages, than the old Soixante-Quinze. Impressed by the performance of the former Mitrailleuse 37mm CA from Schneider, the French command quickly adopted the new 75mm gun.

Springfield Krag-Jørgensen Modelo 1898
Springfield M1898
The Krag-Jørgensen rifle was adopted by the United States in 1892 to replace the ageing Springfield Model 1884. While the Krags proved superior at close ranges to comparable Mauser designs in the Spanish-American war, the rifle's flimsy action in comparison to newer bolt designs necessitated a replacement a mere decade after its adoption. During the American occupations of several Central American nations in the early 20th century, many stockpiled Krag rifles, mostly of the 1898 rifle pattern, were given to local forces along with droves of surplus ammunition.
Carabina del Pueblo Modelo 1934
Carabina M1934
With the socialist revolution, Nicaragua found itself with a rapidly ageing stock of surplus American Krags and little ammunition to supply its meagre arsenal. To alleviate these problems, in 1934, it began an ambitious effort to retrofit and convert its Krag rifles to carbines chambered in 7.5×54mm French, which had successfully been smuggled into the country in large quantities.
Metralleta Modelo 1935
Metralleta M1935
While Nicaragua had yet to formally adopt a submachine prior to the socialist revolution, many Model 1921 Thompsons had entered the country along with the capability of domestic manufacturers to produce .45 ACP ammunition. The Metralleta Model 1935 is a Nicaraguan modified Thompson 1921 with several upgrades and a simplified manufacturing process.
MAS Modèle 1940/42
MAS-40/42
The MAS-40/42 is the latest in a series of Communard semi-automatic rifles. Unlike its predecessors, however, the MAS-40/42 was found to be exceptionally reliable in French military testing. The original five round internal magazine, however, was deemed insufficient and a new variant of the MAS-40/42, the 40/42, was developed featuring a detachable ten round box magazine. A small number of MAS-40/42 rifles were smuggled into Nicaragua along with production tooling specifications.

Rifle, Short, Magazine, Lee-Enfield Mk III
SMLE Mk III
The venerable Lee-Enfield (SMLE) rifle that served the Empire throughout the Great War continues to be the standard arm of the Dominions following the Empire's collapse. Quantities of the SMLE rifle can be found in arsenal throughout the many Dominions. With rifle production seriously disrupted with the loss of the arms factories of England, the Dominions have turned to the old reliable three-oh-three as their standard weapon of choice until suitable replacements are created or purchased.
SMLE Mk V
SMLE Mk V
After the fall of the Home Isles, the need to rearm what remained of the Empire became paramount. British engineers from the RSAF (Royal Small Arms Factory) began taking refuge in Ontario to work on an existing Lee-Enfield. The classic short magazine rifle's updated model displays a new and improved aperture system on the side instead of the middle, which features improved sighting radius, sighting accuracy, and sighting speed. However, due to the foraging and machining operations required to construct the rifle, the production of this new SMLE design is relatively expensive.
Owen Mk 1
Owen Machine Carbine
With the prospect of war coming, the Army has requested a submachine gun to be developed for close quarters and urban warfare, especially with the destructiveness that characterises the civil war in America. The Canadian Army officially adopted a weapon designed by young Australian private Evelyn Owen. The 
Long Branch Mk 1
SLR Mk 1
The Crown Corporation Small Arms Ltd in Long Branch responded to requests from the Canadian government for a self-loading rifle. The self-loading rifle is chambered in 8mm Mauser cartridges and features a tilting bolt action. While the gun suffers from reliability issues and excessive recoil, the rifle comes with a shoulder pad to compensate for the recoil. In addition, the rifle's light weight makes it ideal for mobility and urban combat. Many copies were sent to arm the Dominions of the Empire in preparation for the eventual homecoming.
Receivers, Tabby, Periscope Mk 1
Tabby Scope
The Tabby Scope is one of the Canadian government's most closesly guarded secrets; the process of development is even hidden from the fellow members of the Entente. A chance discovery by scientists in the ISAC, the scope is shaped in the form of a canteen but contains a monocular infrared scope along with a battery. Assigned to only the most elite of the elite, the scope allows the unprecedented advantage of having vision in the dark.
BSA Thompson M1926
The Canadian Army has formally adopted the iconic American submachine gun associated with crime and vice. The M1928A1 is the service variant of the classic M1921 Thompson Machine gun, and although production is expensive, the gun is well-liked among the troops in the field who praise its efficiency and reliability. While most variants rely on smaller 'box' magazines, some other variants feature large and bulky fifty-round drum magazines.
Lanchester Submachine Gun
With the outbreak of all out war in the United States and the looming clouds of war in Europe, the Canadian Army desperately needs arms for the homecoming. With little time to prepare and research, the Canadian Army has commissioned the 'Lanchester' submachine gun, a near perfect copy of the German MP-28.
Canadian Machine Carbine Mk 1
In further cooperation with the Small Arms Ltd at Long Branch, the Canadian military has officially adopted a new compliment and perhaps successor to the Owen. The Machine Carbine Mk 1 features a selective fire trigger with an innovative horizontal magazine to withstand dirt, dust, and sand. Pressure on the top of the trigger would fire the weapon in fully automatic, while pressure on the bottom would fire semi-automatic. While the new machine carbine features a lower rate of fire than the Owen, its reliability and ability to deal selective fire make it quite the potential successor. Many quantities found their way into the arsenal of the Dominions.
Grenade, Rifle, Anti-tank No. 68
Witnessing a demonstration of Henry Mohaupt's anti-tank rifle grenade in Switzerland, a Canadian commission, guessing that the weapon used the shaped charge principle, began work on their own design. The resulting high-explosive anti-tank (HEAT) rifle grenade is hoped to provide greater capabilities for the infantry to fight enemy armour using the cup launchers already in service.
Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I
With the need for a hand-held anti tank weapon for use on the frontlines, the Projector, Infantry, Anti Tank Mk I (PIAT) has been developed based on the design of the bombard mortar. With an effective range of 105 metres in direct fire mode, the device has been well liked due to its lack of backblast - however there has been concerns about its intense recoil.
PIAT
Vickers MG & Stokes mortar
The water cooled Vickers MG and the Stokes trench mortar were the standard support arms of the Canadian Army during the Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the Canadian Army has recommissioned them back into service.
Lewis Mk III DEMS & SBML two-inch mortar
With its iconic 'pan' magazine, the Lewis has served the Tommy since the darkest days of the First Weltkrieg. With the threat of war ever so near, the MKIII variant has a pistol grip that allows it to be fired from the shoulder on any part of a ship's deck. The SBML while small, is incredibly portable and has greater firepower and range than most rifle grenades.
Vickers M Mk II & ML 3 inch mortar
The Mark II variant of the Vickers MG features a selective fire option and a pistol grip. It has been primarily adopted for the armoured vehicles. The ML 3 inch mortar is a considerable upgrade from the Stokes, as it is reliable and sturdy for close artillery support.
Vickers M Mk III & ML 4.2 inch Mortar
The third variant of the esteemed Vickers machine gun was designed for anti-aircraft usage and features a slightly increased rate of fire from its predecessors. Likewise, the 4.2 inch mortar is a heavier upgrade of previous Canadian mortar models and capable of dropping intense and effective support.

Piyade Tüfeği Yapım 1898
Mauser T98
Mauser T98 was the main service rifle of the Ottoman Army during the Weltkrieg. The Ottoman M1903, chambered in 7.65mm, was the most common variant of Mauser in use. Pronounced 'Mavzer' by the Ottomans, this iconic rifle achieved great fame and became deeply ingrained in the Turkish subconscious.\n'One side of me withered from sorrows / the other from a Mavzer wound'
Hümayun Tüfeği Yapım 1355
Kırıkkale T55
Kırıkkale T55 was the name given to the Ottoman modification of Mauser Gewehr 98. Starting in 1933, the Ottoman Empire updated their old rifles to a common configuration known as the Model of 1936 and all in 8x57 Mauser cartridge. The standardisation of all Mausers in Ottoman inventory helped ease the logistical nightmare of having to supply 4 different types of rifle ammunition to all units.
Hümayun Yarı-Otomatik Tüfek Yapım 1360
Kirikkale T60
Kırıkkale T60 Semi Automatic Rifle was based on the receiver of the Gewehr 41, the T-60 is issued with a lighter build and shortened stock, emphasising reliability and speed over range and accuracy. This rifle saw wide use across all branches of service in the Ottoman Military.

Springfield Modelo 1903
Springfield M1903
The Model 1903 was developed at Springfield Arsenal when, following the Spanish-American war, the United States deemed the Krag-Jørgensen rifle inadequate on account of its weak action; at its core, the 1903 was an attempt to combine beneficial features of both the Krag and captured Spanish Mausers. The rifle was gradually adopted along with the .30-06 cartridge both before and while the rest of the world waged the Weltkrieg. Rifles trickled into the hands of Pro-American regimes which controlled Panama until 1924. Panamanian Mausers were rechambered in 7.65×53mm to compliment Panama's Peruvian Mausers.

SME M38
SME M38
Following the rejection of its 1938 pattern semi-automatic rifle, the CZ company attempted to market the design to a variety of foreign nations. The Two Sicilies tested the rifle and like the Austrians, found the rifle unreliable in harsh conditions and difficult to disassemble, but sought to redesign the gun to fix its shortcomings. The SME state run electrical company, which had begun to produce and design small arms only a few years prior, was set to the task. The end result, designated the SME M38 upon adoption, while improved over its Czech predecessor, still bears many of the latter's design quirks.

FS Gruppo 740
FS Gr. 740
Designed by the Adriatic Railways shortly before the nationalisation of the railways in 1905, the 740 Group is a series of 'Consolidation' class locomotives. Built to haul heavy trains on the mountainous lines of the Northern Appennine, while keeping a comparatively low axle load of 14 tons to run on older, less frequently mantained tracks, these engines excelled in their task, and were subsequently built in copious number before the Great War. Distributed across the Peninsula to relieve the older pre-nationalisation engines, a good number of those engines ended up in service around Rome, and subsequently remained in our hands.
FS Gruppo 940
FS Gr. 940
Designed with the 740 Group as a basis, these 'Mikado' class tank engines were built to haul heavy freight trains on the steep lines of the Southern Appennines and the Rome-Pescara line. Though less powerful than their traditional engine-tender counterparts, they are overall more flexible and cheaper to operate, while their lower axle load allows them to run on older tracks.
Mauser Modelo 1907
Modelo 1907
As tensions mounted in the Gran Chaco in the beginning of the 20th century, Bolivia and Paraguay each commenced audacious rearmament campaigns to counter the other. During this period, Paraguay adopted a slightly shortened derivative of the Mauser 1898. This rifle, chambered in 7.65×53mm Mauser, became the backbone of the Paraguayan military until conflict again flared two decades later.
Oviedo Mauser Modelo 1927
Modelo 1927
In 1927, the Spanish Oviedo company won a contract with the Paraguayan government to produce an updated variant of the Argentinian 1909 Mauser. While the rifle itself is top-of-the-line for its time, providing sufficient ammo to supply the rifles has proven surprisingly difficult for Paraguay.

Zbrojovka Brno vz.24
Brno vz.24
The Zbrojovka Brno vz.24 is a carbine based on the German Mauser 1898. While never formally adopted by the Dual Monarchy, the Brno vz.24 saw great success as an export and on the civilian market.
